Let $n \ge 3$ be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:
\begin{itemize}
    \item for all positive integers $a$ and $b$ with $a+b\le n+1$, the point $(a,b)$ lies on at least one of the lines; and
    \item exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny.

- Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (since x+y=0 => y = -x, slope -1). So far, for n=3, possible k are 0 and 1. That suggests that the answer might be that k can be any integer from 0 to n, but with some restrictions. However n=3 is small, and we saw k=1 possible, k=2 impossible. So maybe for larger n, the range expands. Let's test n=4.

We need to determine all k (0 ≤ k ≤ 4) such that there exist 4 distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 5.

Points: s=2..5. For each s, points count s-1. So total points = 1+2+3+4 = 10. That's 10 points. We have 4 lines. We need to cover all 10 points.

We can try to construct examples for various k.

First, note that non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) can cover many points. For n=4, the three special directions are: horizontal lines y=c (c=1..4), vertical lines x=c (c=1..4), and slope -1 lines x+y=c (c=2..5). The maximum number of points on a horizontal line is at most (n+1-c) but for c=1, it's 4 points (since a from 1 to 4, b=1, a+b≤5 → a≤4). For c=2, 3 points; c=3, 2 points; c=4, 1 point. Similarly vertical: x=1 → 4 points; x=2 → 3 points; x=3 → 2 points; x=4 → 1 point. Slope -1: x+y=2 → 1 point (1,1); x+y=3 → 2 points (1,2),(2,1); x+y=4 → 3 points (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); x+y=5 → 4 points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So indeed each of the three families can have lines covering up to n points (for n=4, up to 4 points). The problem says "exactly k of the n lines are sunny". So we have n lines total, some are sunny, some are not. We can choose any lines. So we need to see which k are achievable.

We suspect that maybe all k from 0 to n are possible, but the n=3 case contradicts that (k=2,3 not possible). So perhaps the achievable k are those with 0 ≤ k ≤ n and k ≤ something like floor((n-1)/2) or something. Let's explore.

But note: The problem asks "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ...". So we are to find, given n≥3, the set of possible k. The answer likely depends on n, maybe it's all k except some extreme values. Let's attempt to construct for n=4, k=0 (all lines non-sunny). Is it possible with 4 lines? We need to cover all 10 points with 4 lines, each non-sunny (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). Now k=1: need exactly 1 sunny line. That means 3 non-sunny lines. We already have a construction with 3 non-sunny lines (x=1,y=1,x+y=5) covering 9 points, missing (2,2). We could add a sunny line to cover (2,2) instead of using y=2. But then we would have 4 lines, 1 sunny, 3 non-sunny. The example we gave with y=2 (non-sunny) gave k=0. If we want k=1, we need to replace one of the non-sunny lines with a sunny line. But we need to cover all points with exactly 1 sunny line. Could we have 3 non-sunny lines covering 9 points and then a sunny line covering the missing point? That would be 4 lines with 1 sunny, 3 non-sunny. But we also have the option to use other non-sunny lines, not necessarily the ones we used. Let's try to see if we can cover all points with 3 non-sunny lines. The maximum number of points a non-sunny line can cover is 4 (for n=4). So three such lines could cover at most 12 points, but we have 10 points. However we must consider collinearity: Some points may be covered multiple times, but union can be all. Is it possible to cover all 10 points with 3 lines, each of which is horizontal, vertical, or slope -1? Let's check. The points are in a triangular grid. The lines of these three directions are essentially the "grid lines". The union of all horizontal lines y=1..4 covers all points, but we only have 3 lines. Similarly vertical. Could we pick three lines (maybe two horizontals and one vertical) that cover all points? Let's examine.

The set of points can be described as all integer pairs (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤5. The anti-diagonals (constant a+b) are lines of slope -1. The horizontals are rows, verticals are columns.

L1: x = 1 (vertical) covers points (1,b) with b ≥ 1 and 1 + b ≤ n+1 → b ≤ n.
L2: y = 1 (horizontal) covers points (a,1) with a ≥ 1 and a + 1 ≤ n+1 → a ≤ n.
L3: x + y = n + 1 (slope -1) covers points (a, n+1 - a) with a ≥ 1 and a ≤ n (since b≥1 → a ≤ n). So it covers points with a+b = n+1.

Now consider points with a+b ≤ n. Are they covered by L1 or L2? For a+b ≤ n, the point (a,b) lies on L1 if a = 1, and on L2 if b = 1. If both a > 1 and b > 1, then it is not covered by L1 or L2. Now we have n lines. We want to cover all points. The n lines can be partitioned into sunny and non-sunny. Let S be the set of sunny lines, size k. Let T be the set of non-sunny lines, size n-k. The three families are limited. Each line can cover at most n points. So the maximum union size from m lines is at most (max points per line) * m, but due to overlaps, it's less. To maximize union, we want lines that cover disjoint sets. The maximum number of points we can cover with m lines is something like: we can take m lines from different families that are far apart, but there are only 3 directions. For m=4, we can take 2 lines from one family and 2 from another, etc. However, lines from the same family (e.g., horizontals) can cover disjoint sets if they are far apart (e.g., y=1 and y=2 cover different rows). Similarly verticals. For slope -1 lines, they are parallel, so they are disjoint (they don't intersect). So we can take multiple lines from the same family, each covering a distinct set of points. So we can cover up to (n) points from each line. The sum of sizes of lines can exceed 21, but the union cannot exceed 21. So we can try to cover all points. But with 4 lines, can we cover all points? For n=6, we have 21 points. Let's see if we can cover them with 4 lines. The three families have lines that cover many points. For instance, take:

- y=1 (6 points)
- y=2 (5 points)
- y=3 (4 points)
- y=4 (3 points) but that's 4 lines, all horizontals. Union size = 6+5+4+3 = 18 (since they are disjoint). That covers 18 points. Missing points are those with y≥5? Actually y=5 has 2 points? Wait y=5 has points (1,5),(2,5) (since a+b≤7, y=5 gives a≤2). That's 2 points. y=6 has (1,6). So missing points after taking y=1..4 are: (1,5),(2,5),(1,6). That's 3 points. So complement size 3. That's similar. But maybe with a combination of horizontals and verticals and slope -1 lines we could cover all points. Let's think about the covering problem. The points form a triangle. We can use lines of special directions to cover all points, but the minimum number of such lines needed to cover all points is something like? For n=3, 3 lines suffice. For n=4, 4 lines (3 non-sunny + 1 sunny) but we need to cover with n lines, but we can also use sunny lines. However for k=2, we want exactly 2 sunny lines. That means we have n-2 non-sunny lines. So we need to cover all points with n-2 special lines and 2 sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points each. So the total coverage from non-sunny lines plus sunny lines must be all points. The non-sunny lines are special; they can cover many points. So we need to see for which n we can achieve k=2. Possibly for n large enough, we can choose n-2 non-sunny lines that cover almost all points, leaving a small set that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Let's analyze.

General n≥3. The points P = {(a,b)∈ℕ^+×ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}. Total number of points = 1+2+...+n = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines. Let T be the set of non-sunny lines, |T| = n - k. Each non-sunny line is either horizontal (y = constant), vertical (x = constant), or slope -1 (x+y = constant). We can choose which constants. Sunny lines are arbitrary.

The union of T covers some set of points. The complement U = P \ (union of T) is the set of points that need to be covered by sunny lines. Let s = |U|. Then we need s ≤ 2k, because each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from P (since any line with slope not 0,∞,-1 can contain at most 2 points of P). However is it possible that a sunny line contains more than 2 points from P if its slope is something like 1/2 and it passes through many lattice points within the triangle? Let's analyze more carefully.

Given P is a set of integer lattice points within a right triangle. Consider a line L (in the plane). The intersection L ∩ P is a set of integer points. The number of integer points on a line can be arbitrarily large if the line has rational slope and passes through many lattice points. But here P is bounded and has a specific shape. Could there be a line with slope, say, 1/2 that contains 3 points of P? Let's examine. For n large, P includes many points. Suppose we have a line y = (1/2)x + b. For integer x, y is integer only when x is even. So such a line can contain points with x even. It could contain multiple points within the triangle if the triangle is large. For example, take n=10, P includes points up to a+b ≤ 11. The line y = (1/2)x + 1 could pass through (2,2), (4,3), (6,4), (8,5), (10,6) perhaps, but need to check if those satisfy a+b ≤ 11? (2,2) sum 4 ≤11, (4,3) sum 7, (6,4) sum 10, (8,5) sum 13 >11, so not all. So maybe at most 2 points? But need a rigorous bound.

Observation: For any line L that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, the set of integer points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 that lie on L is at most 2. Is this always true? Let's test with n=5, we saw that the line y = 2x (slope 2) passes through (1,2) and (2,4) (since 2+4=6 ≤6). That's 2 points. Could there be a line with slope 1/2 that passes through (2,2) and (4,3) and (6,4) but (6,4) sum 10 ≤ n+1? For n=5, n+1=6, so (6,4) sum 10 >6, so not in P. For n=10, n+1=11, (10,6) sum 16 >11, so not. So maybe indeed the maximum is 2. But is it possible to have a line with slope, say, 3/2 that passes through (2,3) and (4,6) (sum 10) and (6,9) (sum 15) etc. The triangle restricts a+b ≤ n+1. So the line can intersect the triangle in a segment; the integer points on that line within the triangle are those with a+b ≤ n+1. For a line that is not one of the three special directions, the set of integer points on it within this triangle can be at most 2. Let's try to prove this.

We have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. Suppose a line L (not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) contains three distinct points P1,P2,P3 from this set. Since L is not vertical, we can param. The points are integer lattice points. The slope of L is rational. We can consider the differences. But perhaps there is a more combinatorial argument: The lines of special directions partition the triangle in some way. However we need to be careful. Let's attempt to prove:

Claim: For n≥3, any line L (in the plane) that is not horizontal, not vertical, and not of slope -1 can contain at most 2 points from the set S_n = {(a,b)∈ℕ^+×ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}.

Proof approach: Suppose L contains three points A,B,C ∈ S_n. Then the slope of L is (b2-b1)/(a2-a1) which is rational. The three points are collinear. Consider the lines L and the three special directions. Since L is not parallel to any of those, its slope is different from 0, ∞, -1. We want to derive a contradiction using the bound a+b ≤ n+1. Let's attempt to show that if a line contains three integer points with a+b ≤ n+1, then its slope must be 0, ∞, or -1, or the points are not all within the triangle? That seems plausible because the triangle is "thin". Let's examine.

Take three collinear integer points (a1,b1),(a2,b2),(a3,b3). Consider the vector differences. If the line is not one of the special directions, then the differences have some relation. The condition a+b ≤ n+1 imposes a linear inequality. Perhaps we can consider the projection onto the direction (1,1). The quantity a+b is linear. For a line with slope ≠ -1, the values of a+b along the line are not constant; they change. If the line contains three points, then the a+b values at those points are three distinct numbers (or maybe some equal). But if the line is not parallel to (1,1) direction, then a+b is strictly monotonic along the line. So the three a+b values are distinct. The maximum a+b is ≤ n+1. That's okay.

But why would that limit the number of integer points? There's no obvious bound. For example, consider the line y = 2x. It contains points (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) etc. For n=6, (3,6) sum=9 ≤7? Actually n+1=7, so (3,6) sum=9 >7, so not in S_6. For n=7, n+1=8, (3,6) sum=9 >8, so not. So maybe the triangle's shape prevents three points on a line with slope not special. But is there a line with slope, say, 2/3 that passes through (3,2) and (6,4) and (9,6)? Sums: (3,2)=5, (6,4)=10, (9,6)=15. So if n is large enough (n≥14, n+1≥15), then (9,6) is in S_n. So with n=14, S_14 includes points up to a+b ≤ 15. (9,6) sum=15 ≤15. So we have three points on line y = (2/3)x? But (3,2) and (6,4) and (9,6) are collinear with slope 2/3? Let's compute slope: (4-2)/(6-3) = 2/3. (6-4)/(9-6) = 2/3. So yes. So for n=14, we have three points (3,2),(6,4),(9,6) all in S_14? Check (3,2): 3+2=5 ≤15, ok. (6,4): 6+4=10 ≤15, ok. (9,6): 9+6=15 ≤15, ok. So indeed we have three collinear points on a line with slope 2/3, which is not 0, ∞, -1. So the claim that any line not special can contain at most 2 points from S_n is false for large n. So we need to reconsider.

But note that the problem says "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0." So sunny lines are those with slopes not 0, ∞, -1. But they could be any other slope, possibly rational slopes like 2/3, 1/2, etc. And as we just saw, such a line can contain many lattice points if it passes through many integer points. For S_n (the triangle of points), the maximum number of collinear points with slope not 0,∞,-1 can be more than 2, depending on n. For example, for n=14, we found three points on line y = (2/3)x (but we need to check that the line passes through (3,2),(6,4),(9,6) indeed; but does it also pass through (12,8) etc? That would be further). So the bound is not trivial. However, note that in the problem, we are allowed to choose any lines (including those that are not special). The sunny lines are those not parallel to the axes or x+y=0. So they can have any other slope. So we need to analyze the covering problem with that in mind.

But we earlier derived that with n=3,4,5, k=2 seemed impossible, and we argued that the complement after using some non-sunny lines had 3 points, and any sunny line could cover at most 1 of them because any pair among those points is collinear on a non-sunny direction. That argument used that the complement points (like (2,2),(2,3),(3,2)) are such that any line containing two of them is parallel to one of the three special directions. That's true. For n=6, we considered a set of 4 non-sunny lines that left complement of size 3 (points with a,b≥2 and a+b ≤ 5: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2)). The complement size after using m non-sunny lines is at least something. Let's analyze more systematically.

We have n lines total. Let m = n - k be the number of non-sunny lines. Then we have k sunny lines. We need to cover all P points. We can choose which specific lines. The sunny lines can be any lines; they can cover many points if they have rational slopes that pass through many lattice points. So we cannot simply bound each sunny line by 2. That bound is only for lines that are not special, but we are using sunny lines (which are not special), so they could contain many points. We can treat families separately. There are three families. To maximize union, we want lines that cover disjoint sets. Complement size 3 (since total 21). That's same pattern. Could we get complement size 2 or 1 with 4 lines? Possibly if we take lines that cover more points, but there is a maximum union size. The maximum union from m special lines is achieved by taking the m lines with the largest possible sizes (either all from one family, or a combination). But note that we can also take lines that are not all from the same family but have less overlap. For m=4, we could take 2 horizontals and 2 verticals, but they overlap. The sum of sizes might exceed the union size, but we want to maximize union. The union of m special lines is at most the sum of their sizes minus overlaps. To maximize union, we want lines that are as disjoint as possible. The families are such that lines from the same family are disjoint (horizontals are distinct rows, no intersection; verticals are distinct columns; slope -1 lines are distinct parallels). Lines from different families intersect. So the maximum union from m lines is achieved by taking m lines all from the same family (since that yields the largest sum of sizes with no overlaps). However, we also need to consider that within a family, the sizes decrease as we go away from the "center". For horizontals, the largest sizes are y=1 (n), y=2 (n-1), ... So the sum of the m largest sizes is something like n + (n-1) + ... + (n-m+1) = m(2n - m + 1)/2. For m = n - k. For n=6, m=4, sum = 6+5+4+3 = 18. That's union size. (1,5)-(2,5): horizontal (y=5) → not sunny.
(1,5)-(1,6): vertical (x=1) → not sunny.
(2,5)-(1,6): slope (6-5)/(1-2) = 1/(-1) = -1 → slope -1 (non-sunny). So indeed any pair is non-sunny. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 of them. With 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points covered. So impossible.

Thus for n=6, k=2 impossible. So pattern: For any n≥3, if we take m = n - 2 non-sunny lines (the maximum we can use for k=2), the best we can do is to take the largest m lines from a family, which covers m(2n - m + 1)/2 points, leaving complement size n(n+1)/2 - m(2n - m + 1)/2. Let's compute complement size for m = n - 2. For n≥3, m ≥ 1. Compute:

Total points P_n = n(n+1)/2.

Sum of sizes of m largest horizontals: H_m = n + (n-1) + ... + (n-m+1) = m(2n - m + 1)/2.

Plug m = n - 2:

For a given m, the maximum sum of sizes of m lines (each being a line of a special direction) is not simply the sum of the m largest sizes in that family, because we could also take lines from different families that have large sizes. Now we need to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines covering P. Since we have n lines total, and we can decide how many are sunny. For each k, we can try to construct a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines. If k is large, we need many sunny lines, but then we have fewer non-sunny lines. The complement after using non-sunny lines may be large, and we need to cover it with sunny lines. The sunny lines can be chosen to cover many points. But we need to check the feasibility. The problem likely expects a solution that determines the set of possible k in terms of n, probably something like: all k such that k ≤ n - 2 or k = n? But we saw n=3, k=1 (which is n-2 = 1) works, n=4, k=3 (n-1) works, but k=2 (n-2) fails. So n-2 is not always achievable. For n=3, n-2=1 works. For n=4, n-2=2 fails. For n=5, n-2=3 works? Actually n-2=3, we saw k=3 works. So n-2 works for n=3,5 but not for n=4. So pattern maybe related to parity of n? Let's test n=4: n-2=2 fails. n=5: n-2=3 works. n=6: n-2=4 fails? Actually for n=6, the two largest horizontals are y=1 (6), y=2 (5) => sum 11, union size 11. That leaves complement 10. Then we have 4 sunny lines. Those 4 sunny lines need to cover 10 points. For k = n-2, we have m = 2, which is much smaller. So the number of sunny lines is larger, but we have more flexibility. So we need to consider all k from 0 to n.

Let's approach systematically: We have n lines. Let a = n - k be the number of non-sunny lines. Then we have k sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are from the three families. The sunny lines can be any lines (including those that might be parallel to special directions, but they are not allowed because they would be non-sunny). So sunny lines are not parallel to axes or slope -1.

We need to cover all P points. We can choose which specific lines. The question is: for which k does there exist a set of n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines satisfying covering? So we can attempt to construct for various k. The problem likely expects an answer like: k can be any integer from 0 to n except when k is even and greater than 1? But we need to test more values, maybe all k are possible except when k is even and k ≤ n-2? That seems messy.

Better approach: We can attempt to construct examples for all k from 0 to n, then prove impossibility for some k. The small n experiments show that for n=3, k=2,3 impossible; for n=4, k=2 impossible, but k=3 possible; for n=5, k=2 impossible, k=3 possible; what about k=4 for n=5? That's k = n-1, which is 4. That's a lot of sunny lines. With 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny, can we cover all points? The non-sunny line can be, say, x=1 (covers 5 points). The complement is 10 points. Then we have 4 sunny lines. Are 4 sunny lines enough to cover 10 points? Possibly yes. But we need to check if there is any obstruction. However we should also consider the possibility that some k are impossible because the number of sunny lines is insufficient to cover the uncovered points after using the maximum possible non-sunny lines. But we can also use sunny lines that are not necessarily covering the complement only; they can also cover some points that are already covered, but that doesn't matter. The question is: given n lines, can we cover all P? The lines are distinct. We can choose any set. So we can try to design a configuration where the non-sunny lines are as many as possible (k=0) and then for larger k, we replace some non-sunny lines with sunny lines. But we must maintain covering property. So we can try to see if we can always achieve k=1 (which we already did for n=3,4,5). That's easy. For k=2, we need to see if it's possible for any n≥3. Let's test n=3, impossible; n=4, impossible; n=5, impossible; n=6, likely impossible. So maybe k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need to confirm with a proof. Let's attempt to prove that if k=2, then n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines cannot cover all P. That is, we need to show that with n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement (points not on those lines) has size at least 3, and those points are such that any line containing two of them is non-sunny, so they cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we also have the possibility that the 2 sunny lines are not necessarily covering only the complement; they could cover some points that are also covered by non-sunny lines. The complement is the set of points not covered by the non-sunny lines. To cover all points, we need to cover that complement with the sunny lines. Since each sunny line can contain at most 1 of those points (as argued), we need at least |U| sunny lines. So if |U| ≥ 3, then k ≥ 3. Therefore, if we can prove that for any selection of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement U has size at least 3, then k=2 is impossible. But is it always true that the complement size is at least 3? The maximum union size from n-2 non-sunny lines is at most something, and we can try to bound the complement size from below. However we must consider that the non-sunny lines are not arbitrary; they can be any lines of special directions. The maximum union size achievable with n-2 such lines is something like H_{n-2} = (n-2)(n+3)/2, which is less than n(n+1)/2 by 3. That gives a lower bound on complement size: C ≥ 3. But is it possible that the union size is larger than H_{n-2}? For example, if we take some lines from different families, could the union be larger than the sum of the n-2 largest lines from a single family? The sum of the n-2 largest lines from a family is H_{n-2}. However, we might also take lines that are not the largest in size, but some lines from other families that are larger in size than some of the largest lines in that family. But note that the largest line in any family is size n, the next is n-1, etc. So the sum of the n-2 largest lines in a family is n + (n-1) + ... + (n - (n-2) + 1) = n + (n-1) + ... + 3. That's (n-2)(n+3)/2. That's a specific number. Could we get a larger union by mixing? For instance, if we take all n-2 lines from one family, that's H_{n-2}. If we take some from other families, we might cover some points that are not covered by those lines, but we also might cover fewer points due to overlaps. The union size cannot exceed the total number of points. The complement size is total minus union. The minimal possible complement size is achieved when union is maximal. So we need to find the maximum possible union size with n-2 non-sunny lines. If that maximum is something like (n-2)(n+3)/2, then the complement size is at least 3. But if there is a combination that yields a larger union, say (n-2)(n+3)/2 + something, then complement could be smaller. But can we achieve union size = n(n+1)/2 - 2 or 1? That would require covering almost all points. Let's see for n=6, we found union 18 with 4 lines. That's total 21 - 3. Could we get 19? That would be complement 2. Is it possible with 4 lines? Let's try to cover all points except two. For n=6, total points 21. If we take lines like y=1, y=2, y=3, y=4, that's 4 lines covering 6+5+4+3 = 18. Complement 3. If we try to take y=1, y=2, x=1, x=2, we got union 18 (complement 3). If we take y=1, y=2, y=3, y=4? That's 4 lines, union 6+5+4+3 = 18. That's the same. Could we take y=1, y=2, y=3, x=1? That's 4 lines: y1,y2,y3 sizes 6+5+4=15, plus x1 size 6, overlaps at (1,1) (y1 and x1), (1,2) (y2 and x1), (1,3) (y3 and x1) and maybe (1,? Actually (1,1) y1 and x1, (1,2) y2 and x1, (1,3) y3 and x1. That's 3 overlaps. So union = 15+6 - 3 = 18. Same. So seems the maximum union is 18. Could we get 19 by picking lines like y=1, y=2, y=3, and then some line of slope -1 that covers some new points but also overlaps? But we already used 3 horizontals covering rows 1-3 (sizes 6+5+4 = 15). The complement after those is points with y≥4. That's (1,4),(2,4),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) etc. Actually for n=6, the points with y≥4 are: y=4: (1,4),(2,4),(3,4) → 3 points; y=5: (1,5),(2,5) → 2 points; y=6: (1,6) → 1 point; total 6 points. Adding a line like x=1 would cover some of those, but we already have x=1 is not used. However we have 4 lines; if we use y=1,y=2,y=3 (3 lines) and then another line (maybe slope -1) that covers some new points, but note that the union size after 3 horizontals is 15, complement 6. Adding a line of a special direction can cover at most something. The sum of sizes of that line is at most 6. However overlaps could be large. For example, adding y=4 (size 3) would add 3 new points (since rows 1-3 cover y=1,2,3, and y=4 is disjoint), union becomes 18, complement 3. That's exactly the 4 horizontals case. If we add a line like x=1 (size 6), overlaps with the horizontals: y=1 and x=1 intersect at (1,1); y=2 and x=1 at (1,2); y=3 and x=1 at (1,3). That's 3 overlaps, so union = 15+6-3 = 18. So still 18. Could we add a line of slope -1, like x+y=7 (size 6) that intersects each of the horizontals at points: y=1 ∩ x+y=7 → (6,1) (since 6+1=7), y=2 ∩ x+y=7 → (5,2), y=3 ∩ x+y=7 → (4,3). That's 3 overlaps. So union = 15+6-3 = 18. So indeed any 4th line gives union 18. So maximum is 18. So complement size 3. So for n=6, k=2 impossible. Similarly for n=7, m=5, union with 5 horizontals (y=1..5) sizes 7+6+5+4+3 = 25, total points 28, complement 3. So pattern holds: with m = n-2, maximum union is n(n+1)/2 - 3. So complement size at least 3. Therefore, with exactly k=2 sunny lines, we have m = n-2 non-sunny lines. The complement U has size at least 3. But we need to cover U with 2 sunny lines. As argued, any two points in U are collinear on a non-sunny line (we need to prove that). However is it always the case that U, after selecting m = n-2 non-sunny lines, will be a set of 3 points (or more) such that any line containing two of them is non-sunny? Possibly U could be larger, but still we need to cover it with 2 sunny lines. The obstacle is that each sunny line can cover at most 1 of those points, because if it covered two, then those two points would be collinear on a sunny line, but we need to examine whether that can happen with points in U. However note that U is the complement of the union of the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are of special directions. The points in U are those that are not on any of those lines. Could it be that two points in U happen to lie on a line that is sunny (i.e., not parallel to axes or slope -1)? Possibly, if those points are not aligned on a special direction. But we need to prove that for any selection of n-2 non-sunny lines, the set U (size ≥ 3) has the property that any two points in U are collinear on a line that is not sunny? Actually we need to show that there is no sunny line that contains two points of U. But is that always true? Let's analyze.

We have n-2 non-sunny lines. They are chosen from the families H, V, S (slope -1). The union of these lines covers some points. The complement U is the set of points not covered. Could U contain two points that lie on a sunny line? Suppose we have two points P and Q in U. They are not on any of the chosen non-sunny lines. Could they be collinear with a line that is not special (sunny)? If so, that line would be a candidate sunny line covering both. That would be okay; we could use that sunny line to cover both. So the issue is not that any two points in U cannot be on a sunny line, but that we need to cover all points in U with only k sunny lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. If U has size 3, then we need to cover 3 points with 2 lines. Even if two of those points can be covered by one sunny line, and the third by another, that would be 2 lines covering 3 points. That's possible if there exists a sunny line covering two of them. But we need to see whether such a configuration exists with the non-sunny lines chosen appropriately. So the impossibility claim for k=2 is not automatically that U has size ≥ 3 and that any line containing two of U is non-sunny; we need to prove that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement U (size at least 3) is such that no sunny line can contain two points of U. But is it possible to choose non-sunny lines that leave a U of size 3 where two of those points are collinear on a sunny line? Let's examine the structure of P.

We have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The points that are not covered by a particular set of such lines might be located in a certain region. For example, if we take the n-2 largest horizontals, U consists of points with y≥n-1 (since y=1..n-2 are covered). That's a small set. Let's compute U for n=6, m=4, using y=1,2,3,4 (horizontals). U = {(1,5),(2,5),(1,6)}. Are any two of these on a sunny line? (1,5)-(2,5) is horizontal, not sunny. (1,5)-(1,6) vertical, not sunny. (2,5)-(1,6) slope -1, not sunny. So indeed no sunny line covers two. That's because U is a set of points that are all in the "upper right" region and are arranged such that any two are either vertical, horizontal, or slope -1. This seems to be a general phenomenon: The points that are not covered by the largest m horizontals (y=1..m) are those with y > m. But note that the triangle's shape: For y > m, the x-coordinate is limited. Those points are such that any two of them are either on a horizontal (if same y) or vertical (if same x) or slope -1? Let's analyze.

Suppose we take the m largest horizontals. Then the complement U is the set of points with y ≥ m+1. These points have coordinates (a,b) with b ≥ m+1, a ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1. For such points, the differences in a and b are limited. The set of points with b ≥ m+1 is a small triangle at the top. In such a set, any two points are either with different b and a. Let's try to find two points in U that are collinear on a sunny line. For example, for n=7, m=5, U after taking y=1..5 (horizontals) would be points with y=6,7. y=6: (1,6),(2,6) sum7, (3,6) sum9>8? Actually n=7 => n+1=8, so y=6 => a≤2 (since 6+2=8). So points (1,6),(2,6). y=7: (1,7). That's three points. Are any two of these on a sunny line? (1,6)-(2,6) horizontal; (1,6)-(1,7) vertical; (2,6)-(1,7) slope (7-6)/(1-2)=1/(-1) = -1. So none sunny. So indeed not.

Now could there be a different choice of non-sunny lines (like a mix) such that U has size 3 but two of those points lie on a sunny line? For instance, take n=6, m=4. We saw that taking y=1,2,3,4 gives U = {(1,5),(2,5),(1,6)}. Could we choose 4 lines that are not all horizontals, but still cover 18 points, and leave a U of size 3 that is different, maybe with points that are not all on special lines? But the maximum union size is 18; any set of 4 special lines can cover at most 18 points, leaving at least 3 uncovered. So U has size at least 3. The structure of U depends on which lines are chosen. But we need to show that for any selection of 4 special lines, the uncovered points are such that no sunny line can contain two of them. However is it possible that U has size 3 but the three points are, say, (2,2), (2,3), (3,2) which we saw are all on non-sunny lines? But those points are covered by some special lines; if we choose non-sunny lines that cover all other points, then U might be exactly those three. For n=5, we had U = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)} with m=3. That's size 3. And indeed those three points are such that any two are on a non-sunny line. For n=6, if we take m=4, could we get U = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}? That's size 3. But is that possible? Let's test. For n=6, the points with a,b≥2 and a+b≤5 are (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) (and also (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) are the only such with a+b≤5). But there are also points with a+b=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So if we cover all points except those three, we would have union size 21-3 = 18. So we need to cover 18 points with 4 special lines. Can we cover all points except those three? That would mean that the union of the 4 lines contains all points except (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). But is that possible? Let's try to see if there is a set of 4 special lines that covers all points except those three. For n=6, the three points (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) are in the lower right region. They are not on the "border" of the triangle. Could we choose lines like y=1,2,3,4 (horizontals) covering rows 1-4, which leaves points with y≥5 (which are (1,5),(2,5),(1,6) etc). That doesn't cover (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) because those have y=2,3, so they are covered by y=2,y=3. So those are not uncovered. So to leave those three uncovered, we need to avoid covering them. But we have 4 lines; we could try to cover all points except those three. But can we cover all other points? For example, take lines: y=1, y=4, x=1, x=5? That seems unlikely. Let's systematically consider the possibilities.

We need to see whether there exists a selection of n-2 non-sunny lines that results in U being a set of points where at least two are collinear on a sunny line. But perhaps it's easier to prove that for any selection of m = n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement U has size at least 3, and moreover U is a subset of the points that are not on the "border" of the triangle, but we need a property that any line passing through two points of U is non-sunny. However, is it always true that for any selection of m special lines, U is contained in the set of points that are "far from the axes" and thus any two are on a non-sunny line? Let's think.

The complement U is the set of points not covered by the chosen special lines. Since the special lines are axis-aligned or slope -1, they cover many points. The uncovered points are those that avoid all those lines. If we choose lines that are all from one family, U is a small set at the top or right or diagonal. That set has the property that any two points are on a line that is not sunny (as we saw). But if we choose a different combination, U could be a different set. For instance, if we choose 3 lines: y=1, y=2, x=1 (3 lines) and then we need a 4th line, but we are considering m=4, so we could choose some lines that are not all from same family. The complement might be something like {(3,3),(4,2),(5,1)} maybe? But that seems unlikely because with 4 lines we can cover at most 18 points. The complement size is at least 3. The question is: is it possible that U contains three points such that two of them are not on a special direction, i.e., they lie on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1? If so, then with 2 sunny lines, we could cover those points (one sunny line covering two, the other covering the third). So we need to examine whether for n≥3, there exists a set of n-2 special lines such that the complement U has size 3 and those 3 points are not all pairwise on special lines. But we also need to cover the entire P, so U must be covered by sunny lines. If U is of size 3, we need at least 2 sunny lines, but we have exactly 2 sunny lines. So if there exists a sunny line covering two of them, then the union of that sunny line and another sunny line covering the third would cover U. So it's plausible. But is it possible with the constraints of covering? We need to also ensure that the union of the non-sunny lines covers all points except those three. That's a covering problem. Let's attempt to construct for n=6, m=4, to cover all points except (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Can we find 4 special lines that cover the other 18 points? Let's list all points P for n=6 (21 points). The points we want to leave uncovered are U0 = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}. We need to see if there exists a set T of 4 lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) such that P \ T = U0. That is, T covers all points except those three. Equivalently, U0 must be disjoint from T. So T must not contain any of those points. Also T must cover all other points. So we need to find 4 lines that avoid covering those points, but still cover all other points. Is that possible? Let's try to design.

We can try to use lines that are "far" from those points. For instance, take y=1 (covers (1,1)-(6,1)). That covers (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1) and (1,1). That's okay. It does not cover (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's fine. Next, y=2 (covers (1,2)-(5,2)). That covers (2,2) and (3,2). That's not allowed because we want to leave those uncovered. So y=2 would cover them. So we cannot use y=2. Similarly, y=3 covers (1,3)-(4,3). That includes (2,3) (since 2+3=5 ≤7). So cannot use y=3. y=4 covers (1,4)-(3,4). That includes (2,4) not in U0. That's okay. But we need to cover all points except U0. So we need to cover points like (2,4), (3,3), etc. The issue is that we cannot use some horizontals because they would cover U0. So we might use a different family. Maybe we can use some vertical lines. x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,6). That includes (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6). That's fine; does not cover U0. x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,5). That includes (2,2),(2,3) -> U0 covered. So cannot use x=2. x=3 covers (3,1)-(3,4). That includes (3,2) -> U0 covered. So cannot use x=3. x=4 covers (4,1)-(4,3). That includes (4,2) not in U0, but also (4,3) maybe. That's okay. So we have constraints. We need 4 lines. Could we take y=1, y=4, x=1, and some slope -1 line like x+y=7 (covers (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1)). Does that cover all points except U0? Let's compute union.

Thus it seems difficult to achieve a complement of size 3 that is not the "top" region. But we need a proof that for any set of n-2 special lines, the complement U has size at least 3, and moreover any two points in U are collinear on a line that is not sunny (i.e., any line containing two points of U is parallel to one of the three special directions). However we saw that for n=6, if we take the four largest horizontals, U consists of points with y≥5. Those are (1,5),(2,5),(1,6). Any two are on non-sunny lines. So that's fine. But could there be a set of 4 special lines that results in U being, say, {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)} (size 3) but also maybe some other points? That would require that those three points are not covered, and all other points are covered. But we argued that to cover all points except those three, we would need a set of 4 special lines that covers all other points. Is that possible? Let's attempt to see if there is a covering with 4 special lines that leaves exactly those three uncovered. For n=6, the points are 21. We need union of 4 lines to have size 18. That's possible. The three uncovered points must be such that they are not on any of the lines. So we need to find 4 lines (horizontals, verticals, slope -1) that avoid covering (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) but cover all other points. Is that plausible? Let's think about the geometry. The points (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) are located at the "inner" region. To cover all points except those, the lines would need to cover the border points, but the inner points are not covered. However many lines that cover border points also cover some inner points. For instance, a horizontal line y=2 covers (2,2) and (3,2) and (1,2),(4,2),(5,2) (but (4,2) is in P). So that would cover those inner points. So we cannot use y=2. Similarly, x=2 covers (2,2),(2,3) etc. So we need to cover all points except those inner ones. The only way to cover points like (1,2) (which is on y=2) without covering (2,2) is to use a line that passes through (1,2) but not through (2,2). However special lines are axis-aligned or slope -1. If we want to cover (1,2) (which is on y=2) we could use a vertical line x=1, which covers (1,2) and also (1,1),(1,3), etc. That's fine. But we also need to cover points like (2,4) (which is on y=4) etc. Let's try to construct a set of 4 lines that covers all points except U0. Perhaps we can use lines:

They could cover at most something. For n=6, the inner points (x≥2,y≥2) are 10 points. With 2 special lines, can we cover all 10? That seems impossible. But we are trying to find a covering with 4 lines that leaves U0 (3 points) uncovered. That would mean that the union of the 4 lines covers all other points. So we need to find 4 lines covering 18 points. The combination we tried (y=1, y=4, x=1, x+y=7) gave only 18 covered, but we saw it missed some points (like (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2),(2,4)? Actually we computed uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2) and also (2,4) maybe? We had (2,4) covered? Let's recompute with that selection: y=1, y=4, x=1, x+y=7. Did we cover (2,4)? (2,4) is on y=4, which we have, so yes. (3,3) is not covered. (4,2) not covered. So uncovered set was 5 points. So not 3. So that selection covers 16 points (since total 21, uncovered 5). So that's not maximum.

Thus to achieve complement size 3, we need to select lines that cover 18 points. The maximum we found is 18 with, say, 4 horizontals (y=1-4). That covers rows 1-4, leaving rows 5-6 uncovered (3 points). That's a specific complement. Could there be another selection covering 18 points with a complement of size 3 that is not those top points? Possibly, but we need to examine the structure. The complement after selecting m special lines is the set of points that are not on any of those lines. The union size is maximized by taking lines from a single family that are as large as possible. That gives complement size = total - sum_{i=1}^{m} (n - i + 1) = n(n+1)/2 - m(2n - m + 1)/2. For m = n-2, we computed complement size 3. So any set of n-2 special lines that achieves union size 18 (for n=6) must have union size at least 18. But is it possible to have union size 18 with a different selection, not all from same family, but still covering 18 points? The sum of sizes of m lines from the same family is H_m = (n-2)(n+3)/2. For n=6, that's 4*9/2 = 18. So that's maximum. Any other combination would have either smaller sum of sizes or more overlaps, thus union size ≤ H_m. So indeed the maximum union size is H_m. Therefore, any set of n-2 special lines covers at most H_m points, leaving at least 3 uncovered. So U has size at least 3. That's a lower bound.

Now, could U have size > 3? Possibly, but then we have more uncovered points. With k=2 sunny lines, we need to cover U. If U has size 3, we need to see if it's possible to cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines. As we saw, if U is exactly the set of points with y≥m+1 after taking the largest m horizontals, then those points are such that no sunny line contains two of them. So covering requires at least 3 sunny lines. But if there exists a different selection of n-2 lines that yields U of size 3, but those 3 points are not all on special lines, maybe they can be covered by 2 sunny lines. However we need to prove that for any selection of n-2 special lines, the uncovered set U (size ≥ 3) has the property that any two points of U are collinear on a non-sunny line. Is that always true? Not obviously. Let's test with n=6, m=4. The maximum union size is 18. So any covering with 4 special lines will cover at most 18 points, leaving at least 3 uncovered. Could there be a covering that covers exactly 18 points but leaves a U of size 3 that is not the top region? For example, if we take lines: y=1, y=2, x=1, x+y=7 (that's 4 lines). That's not all from same family. Let's compute its union size. We'll compute quickly: y1 (6), y2 (5) => sum 11. x1 (6) => total 17, but overlaps: y1 ∩ x1 (1,1); y2 ∩ x1 (1,2). Also x+y=7 intersects each? y1∩x+y=7 → (6,1); y2∩x+y=7 → (5,2); x1∩x+y=7 → (1,6). So overlaps. The union size might be something. Let's compute systematically for n=6.

We need to see if there is any selection of 4 special lines that covers 18 points. The maximum is 18. That's achieved by taking 4 horizontals (y=1,2,3,4). Also by taking 4 verticals (x=1,2,3,4). Also by taking 4 slope -1 lines (c=2,3,4,5? Actually slope -1 lines have sizes 1,2,3,4,5,6 for c=2..7. Taking the four largest (c=4,5,6,7) gives sizes 3+4+5+6 = 18. So indeed those are the only ways to achieve maximum. But we need to consider that we might also take a combination that yields union size 18 even if not all from same family, but the sum of sizes might exceed H_m, but overlaps reduce union. To get union 18, we need the sum of sizes minus overlaps to be 18. The maximum possible sum of sizes for m=4 lines is 4*n = 24, but overlaps are significant. The maximum union size is indeed H_m. So any set of 4 special lines that covers 18 points must have union size 18, which is the maximum. But are there sets that achieve 18 that are not all from one family? For example, taking y=1 (6), y=2 (5), y=3 (4), and y=4 (3) gives 18. That's all horizontals. If we take y=1, y=2, y=3, and some line that is not a horizontal but still covers many points, maybe we could also get 18. We can attempt to characterize the maximum union size and the shape of the complement. Since the total points are n(n+1)/2, and the maximum union from m lines is achieved by taking the m largest lines from a single family. But as we saw, mixing can also achieve maximum. So we need a more systematic argument: For any set T of m special lines, |∪T| ≤ m(2n - m + 1)/2. Equality occurs iff T consists of lines that are all from the same family and are the m largest lines in that family, OR possibly also some combinations that happen to have the same sum. But we should examine the structure of the bound. The bound derived by considering the sizes of lines and overlaps is not trivial, but we can prove a stronger bound: For any selection of m lines (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1), the number of points covered is at most (n + (n-1) + ... + (n-m+1)) = m(2n - m + 1)/2. This seems plausible because the maximum coverage is achieved by taking the m largest lines from a single family. But is it always true that any selection of m lines from the three families covers at most that many points? Let's try to prove.

Let T be a set of m lines, each is of type H (y = c), V (x = c), or S (x+y = c). For each such line L, let size(L) be the number of points from P that lie on L. The union size is at most sum_{L∈T} size(L) minus overlaps. But we cannot directly bound sum size(L) because overlaps can be large. However we can bound the union by the sum of the sizes of the m lines with the largest possible sizes, but that's not rigorous unless we argue that any line's size is ≤ n, and that if we have overlaps, the union is smaller. The key is to design a covering with exactly k sunny lines. Since we have k sunny lines, we can potentially cover many points. The challenge is to prove that for any k with 3 ≤ k ≤ n-1, such a configuration exists. And also to prove impossibility for k=2 and k=n. For k=n, all lines sunny. We need to show that no set of n sunny lines can cover P. For k=2, we need to show that any set of n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines cannot cover P. That seems plausible.

But we also need to consider k > n? k is at most n, so that's covered.

Thus the solution likely is: k can be any integer from 0 to n inclusive except 2 and n. For n=3, that's {0,1}. For n≥4, that's {0,1,3,4,…,n-1}. But we need to be careful: For n=4, n-1=3 works, so allowed. For n=5, n-1=4 works. So the set is {0,1} ∪ {3,4,…,n-1}. That's n-1 possible values (since 2 and n are excluded). So the answer is that all k except k=2 and k=n are possible.

Now we need to produce a rigorous solution. Let's outline:

1. Define P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}. |P_n| = n(n+1)/2.

2. Let S be a set of n distinct lines L_1,…,L_n in the plane such that for each (a,b)∈P_n, there exists i with (a,b)∈L_i. Moreover, exactly k of these lines are sunny. We need to find all k (depending on n) for which such S exists.

We will prove:

- If k = 0 or k = 1, then such S exists. (Construct)
- If 3 ≤ k ≤ n-1, then such S exists.
- If k = 2 or k = n, then such S does not exist.

We'll need to handle n≥3.

Let's start with the existence part.

Construction for k=0: Take the n lines to be the horizontals y = 1,2,…,n. All are non-sunny. They cover all points (since for each a≥1, b≥1, a+b≤n+1, b≤n, so (a,b) lies on y=b). So that's valid. So k=0 is possible.

Construction for k=1: We need exactly 1 sunny line. Take the lines to be: the n-1 horizontals y = 1,2,…,n-1, and one sunny line L (e.g., y = x). But does L cover the missing points? The union of horizontals y=1..n-1 covers all points with b ≤ n-1. Points with b = n are those with a≥1, a+n ≤ n+1 → a ≤ 1. So only (1,n) is in P_n with b=n. But also points with b = n? Actually for a≥1, b=n, a+b ≤ n+1 → a ≤ 1. So (1,n) is the only point with y=n. Also points with b > n are not in P_n. So after taking horizontals y=1..n-1, the uncovered points are those with b = n (only (1,n)). That's one point. The line y = x passes through (1,1) but not through (1,n) unless n=1. So we need a sunny line that passes through (1,n). For n≥3, (1,n) is not on y=x. So we can choose a sunny line that passes through (1,n) and has slope not 0,∞,-1, e.g., the line with slope 1 (y = x + (n-1)). That line passes through (1,n) (since 1+(n-1)=n). That's sunny. But does it pass through any other points in P_n? That's okay. So we can use L: y = x + (n-1). That's sunny. Then the union of horizontals y=1..n-1 and this L covers all points. Are these lines distinct? Yes. So k=1 works for any n≥2? For n=3, that's 2 horizontals (y=1,2) and L: y = x+2 covers (1,3). That's good. For n=2 (but n≥3, so fine). So that's a construction. But we also need to ensure that L is not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0, which it isn't. So that's fine.

Thus k=0,1 always possible.

Now for 3 ≤ k ≤ n-1. We need a construction with exactly k sunny lines. Let m = n - k be the number of non-sunny lines. Since k ≥ 3, m ≤ n-3. We want to cover P_n with m non-sunny lines and k sunny lines. We have freedom to choose which lines. One idea is to use the m largest horizontals (y = n, n-1, …, n-m+1). That's m lines, all non-sunny. Their union covers the points with b ≥ n-m+1. The remaining points are those with b ≤ n-m. That's a triangular region of size about (n-m)(n-m+1)/2. We then need to cover those with k sunny lines. Since k = n - m, and we have n - m sunny lines. That seems like a lot. But is it enough? We need to construct a set of k sunny lines that cover all points with b ≤ n-m. That's a smaller triangle. Since we have many sunny lines, we can try to use lines that are "grid lines" with slope 2, 1, etc., but we need to guarantee coverage. Another approach is to use the following: Take the non-sunny lines to be the m largest vertical lines x = n, n-1, …, n-m+1. That's similar. Then the uncovered points are those with x ≤ n-m. That's also a triangular region. Then we need to cover that with k sunny lines. That seems symmetrical.

But we also have the possibility to use slope -1 lines. Perhaps it's easier to use a mix: for example, for any n, we can take the n-2 largest horizontals (which are non-sunny) and then 2 sunny lines to cover the top region (as we did for n=6, k=3). But we need to cover all points. For k≥3, we have many sunny lines, so we can use a construction where we take a certain number of non-sunny lines (say, m) and then cover the remaining with sunny lines. The challenge is to show that such a covering exists for all m ≤ n-3. Since we have many sunny lines, we can use lines that are "diagonals" of slope 2, 1, etc., to cover the points.

We need to produce a general construction. Let's think about covering a triangular grid with lines that are not special. There is a known technique: For each point (a,b) with a≥1,b≥1,a+b≤n+1, we can consider the line through (a,b) and (a+1,b+1) which has slope 1. That's one line. But we need many lines. We could adjust lines. Maybe we can cover all with 4 lines, but it's not obvious. The example for n=5,k=4 is supposed to be possible; we need to construct. Let's think of a general construction.

Alternative approach: Instead of using m smallest horizontals, we could use m smallest verticals, but that's symmetric. The challenge is to cover a right triangle of size t with t sunny lines. For t=3, we succeeded. For t=4, we need to see if it's possible. If it's not possible, then our claim that all k from 3 to n-1 are possible might be false for some n. But maybe we can use a different set of non-sunny lines that leave a different uncovered triangle that is easier to cover. For example, we could take the non-sunny lines to be some mixture that leaves a small uncovered region, but we have only m non-sunny lines. The uncovered region after taking m lines will be the complement of the union of those lines. To minimize the uncovered region, we would want the m lines to cover as many points as possible. The maximum union size from m lines is H_m. If we take m = n - k, then the uncovered region size is n(n+1)/2 - H_m. For k between 3 and n-1, m ≤ n-3. Let's compute the uncovered size for m = n - t (where t = k). That's C = n(n+1)/2 - (n - t)(2n - (n - t) + 1)/2 = n(n+1)/2 - (n - t)(n + t + 1)/2? Let's compute: m = n - t, then the sum of m largest horizontals (or verticals) is (n - t)(2n - (n - t) + 1)/2 = (n - t)(n + t + 1)/2. So C = [n(n+1) - (n - t)(n + t + 1)] / 2. Expand: n(n+1) = n^2 + n. (n - t)(n + t + 1) = (n - t)(n + t + 1) = (n)(n + t + 1) - t(n + t + 1) = n^2 + n t + n - t n - t^2 - t = n^2 + n - t^2 - t. Wait compute carefully:

(n - t)*(n + t + 1) = (n - t)*(n + t) + (n - t)*1 = (n^2 - t^2) + (n - t) = n^2 - t^2 + n - t.

Thus C = [ (n^2 + n) - (n^2 - t^2 + n - t) ] / 2 = [ n^2 + n - n^2 + t^2 - n + t ] / 2 = [ t^2 + t ] / 2 = t(t+1)/2.

So indeed, if we take the m largest horizontals (or verticals), the uncovered region has size t(t+1)/2. That's the same as the size of T_t. So that's good.

But note: In our earlier construction for n=6,k=3 (t=3), we used m=3 non-sunny lines that were the smallest horizontals (y=1,2,3), not the largest. That's because we wanted to cover the bottom rows, leaving a small triangle at the top. That's also a maximum-union set, but with different ordering. The size of the union of the m smallest horizontals (y=1..m) is also H_m, because those rows are disjoint and have sizes n, n-1, ..., n-m+1. That's the same as the m largest rows (y=n, n-1, ..., n-m+1) because the sizes are decreasing. So either set gives union size H_m. So we can choose whichever is convenient. In the n=6,k=3 example, we used y=1,2,3 (smallest) as non-sunny, leaving the top triangle (y≥4) of size 6. That's exactly the same as using y=4,5,6 as non-sunny would leave bottom triangle of size 6. So both are valid. So we have flexibility.

Thus, for any t ≥ 3, we need to cover T_t (the triangle of points with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ t+1) with t sunny lines. But we already have an example for t=3. For t=4, we need to find a covering. Let's attempt to construct one.

Let's systematically analyze the covering problem. We have P_n of size N = n(n+1)/2. We have n lines. Some are sunny. The non-sunny lines are limited to three directions. The union of those lines can cover at most H_m points, where m is the number of non-sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover some points. The question is, for which k does there exist a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering P_n. The existence construction we have for k=0,1, and also we have a construction for k=3 for n≥4 (by taking m = n-3 non-sunny horizontals and then covering the top triangle with 3 sunny lines). That works for any n≥4. But for n=4, k=3 works. For n=5, k=3 works. For n=6, k=3 works. So k=3 is always possible for n≥4. For n=3, k=3 impossible. So we have a construction for k=3 for n≥4. For k=4, we need to see if it's possible for some n. For n=5, k=4 impossible. For n=6, k=4 impossible? We argued that with m=2 non-sunny, uncovered triangle is T_4 (size 10), and with 4 sunny lines impossible. But maybe we can choose a different set of 2 non-sunny lines that leaves a triangle of size less than 10? The maximum union size with 2 lines is 5+4=9 (if we use horizontals). That's H_2 = 9. That leaves uncovered size 15-9=6. That's T_3? Actually n=6, total 21. If we use 2 non-sunny lines that achieve union 9, uncovered size 12. That's not a triangle of size 10; that's larger. The optimal is to achieve union H_m, which is maximum. That yields uncovered size = N - H_m. For m = n - k. So to minimize uncovered size, we want m as small as possible? Wait we are trying to construct for a given k. We have m = n - k non-sunny lines. The uncovered size is at least N - H_m. If we choose non-sunny lines that achieve H_m, then uncovered size is exactly N - H_m = (k^2 + k)/2. So if we can cover that uncovered set with k sunny lines, then we succeed. So the existence of a configuration for k is equivalent to: There exists a selection of m = n - k non-sunny lines (from the three families) that cover exactly H_m points (max), and then the remaining points (size = k(k+1)/2) can be covered by k sunny lines. But we also need the sunny lines to be distinct and not parallel to axes or slope -1. The uncovered set after optimal non-sunny lines is a right triangle of size k(k+1)/2. So the problem reduces to: For which k does there exist a set of k sunny lines that cover all points in T_k (the triangle of points with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ k+1)? (Because after translation, the uncovered set is T_k). So we need to determine for which k ≥ 0, T_k can be covered by k sunny lines. For k=0, trivial (0 lines cover empty set). For k=1, T_1 has 1 point (1,1). A sunny line (any line not special) can pass through it, e.g., y = x covers (1,1). So possible. For k=2, T_2 has 3 points. Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from T_2 (since max per line is 2). With 2 lines, max union is 4, but we need to cover 3 points. Is it possible? Let's test. T_2 points: (1,1), (1,2), (2,1). We need to cover them with 2 sunny lines. Can we cover all three? Let's attempt: line through (1,1) and (1,2) is vertical, not allowed. line through (1,1) and (2,1) is horizontal, not allowed. line through (1,2) and (2,1) is slope -1, not allowed. So any line containing two points of T_2 is not sunny. Therefore, each sunny line can contain at most one point from T_2. Thus, with 2 lines, we can cover at most 2 points, leaving one uncovered. So impossible. So k=2 impossible. For k=3, T_3 has 6 points. Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points? Actually we saw that a sunny line can cover 2 points from T_3 (e.g., y=x covers (1,1),(2,2); y=-2x+6 covers (1,4),(2,2) but (1,4) not in T_3; (1,3) and (2,2) maybe). But we need to cover all 6 points with 3 lines. The example we gave for T_3 (after translation) used lines: y = x (covers (1,1),(2,2)); y = -2x+5 (covers (2,1),(1,3)); y = -1/2 x + 2.5 (covers (1,2),(3,1)). That's three lines covering all 6 points. So k=3 possible. For k=4, T_4 has 10 points. Each sunny line can cover at most 2 points (as argued). 4 lines can cover at most 8 points, insufficient. So k=4 impossible. For k=5, T_5 has 15 points. Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points (as argued). 5 lines can cover at most 15 points if each covers 3 and they are disjoint. So it's possible in principle. We need to construct an example. For k=6, T_6 has 21 points. Each sunny line can cover at most? The maximum number of points from T_6 on a sunny line is floor((t+1)/2) where t = k = 6? Actually T_k has points with a+b ≤ k+1. A line with slope 1 (y=x) passes through points where a=b, and a ≤ (k+1)/2. So maximum is floor((k+1)/2). For k=6, that's 3. So each sunny line can cover at most 3 points. 6 lines can cover at most 18 points, insufficient. So k=6 impossible. For k=7, T_7 has 28 points. Each sunny line can cover at most floor((7+1)/2)=4 points. 7 lines can cover at most 28 points. So possible. So the pattern is: For k odd? Actually we need to see the threshold. The maximum coverage per line is m = floor((k+1)/2). So the maximum total coverage from k sunny lines is k * floor((k+1)/2). For k = 3, that's 3*2=6, which equals |T_3|. For k = 4, that's 4*2=8 < 10. For k = 5, that's 5*3=15 = |T_5|. For k = 6, 6*3=18 < 21. For k = 7, 7*4=28 = |T_7|. So it seems that when k is odd, k * floor((k+1)/2) = k * ((k+1)/2) = k(k+1)/2 = |T_k|. When k is even, k * (k/2) = k^2/2 < k(k+1)/2. So the necessary condition for covering T_k with k sunny lines is that the sum of the maximum per line times k is at least |T_k|, but also we need to ensure that such a covering exists, i.e., we can select lines that achieve those maxima and are disjoint (or cover all points). However, even if the maximum sum equals |T_k|, it's not automatically achievable because the lines might not be able to cover all points without overlap. But we have examples for k=3, and presumably for all odd k≥3, we can cover T_k with k sunny lines. For k=1, it's also odd and works. For k=5, we can attempt to construct similarly. We need to solve a combinatorial geometry / covering problem. Let me restate:

We have n >= 3 given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
- For all positive integers a and b with a+b <= n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
- Exactly k of the n lines are sunny.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (since x+y=0 => y = -x, slope -1). Actually "parallel to the line x+y=0" means lines of slope -1, i.e., lines of the form y = -x + c. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal (slope 0), not vertical (infinite slope), and not with slope -1. So they can be any other slope, and also can be any line (including those that are not parallel to those three). However note that they must be distinct lines.

We need to determine all possible k (nonnegative integers) for which there exists a set of n distinct lines (in the plane) with exactly k sunny lines, and such that every point (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b <= n+1 lies on at least one of those lines.

Interpretation: The set of points P = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ | a+b ≤ n+1}. That's like a triangular array of lattice points, with a and b starting at 1, up to a+b ≤ n+1. So there are many points: For s = a+b, s ranges from 2 to n+1. For each s, there are (s-1) points (a from 1 to s-1, b = s-a). So total number of points is sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = (n)(n+1)/2? Actually sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = sum_{t=1}^{n} t = n(n+1)/2. That's the triangular number.

We have n lines. Each line can cover many points, but must cover all these points (the union of lines must contain all these points). The lines are distinct. We can choose any lines (maybe not passing through lattice points? But points are lattice points; lines can be any lines, and they will contain points if they pass through them. So we need to cover all these lattice points.

We also have a condition about sunny lines: exactly k of the n lines are sunny. The rest (n-k) lines are not sunny, meaning they are parallel to at least one of the x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. But note that "sunny" is defined as "not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0". So a line is not sunny if it is parallel to at least one of those three directions. That is, it is horizontal (parallel to x-axis), vertical (parallel to y-axis), or with slope -1 (parallel to x+y=0). So non-sunny lines can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They could also be coincident? But lines are distinct. So we have n lines, some of which are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1; exactly k are not of those types (i.e., they have slopes different from 0, infinite, -1). However it's also possible that a line is parallel to more than one of those? But horizontal is not parallel to vertical, and not parallel to slope -1. Similarly vertical is not parallel to slope -1. So lines are classified into three types (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) and then "other" (sunny). But careful: A line could be parallel to both x-axis and y-axis? No, only one direction at a time, unless it's a point? So indeed lines are either horizontal (slope 0), vertical (undefined slope), slope -1, or other slopes.

Thus we need to find all k such that we can select n lines (distinct) with exactly k sunny (non-special) and n-k non-sunny (special) such that all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1 are covered.

We need to determine all possible k (nonnegative integers) for which this exists. Since n ≥ 3, and we have n lines, we can possibly cover many points. The question is: what restrictions on k arise from the covering requirement? Possibly some k are impossible because the non-sunny lines can cover at most certain points, and we need to cover all points with n lines. But we can also choose sunny lines to cover many points. The problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions. So we need to find the set of k (depending on n) for which such a configuration exists.

We should attempt to construct examples for various k, and also prove that for some k it's impossible. The solution likely involves a combinatorial argument about covering a triangular grid with lines. Let's think.

We have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. That's like a right triangle of points. The lines we can use are arbitrary. The points lie on integer lattice. We can consider lines that are "grid lines" like x = 1, x = 2, ..., x = n; y = 1, ..., n; or lines of slope -1 passing through points like (a,b) such that a+b = constant. Indeed a line of slope -1 has equation y = -x + c. The points on such a line with a,b positive integers satisfy a+b = c. So the set of points with a+b = constant is a line of slope -1 (if we consider all real points). So we can cover many points by such lines. Similarly, horizontal lines y = constant cover points with that y-coordinate; vertical lines x = constant cover points with that x-coordinate.

So the natural candidate lines to cover the triangular array are the lines x = 1,2,…,n; y = 1,2,…,n; and lines of slope -1 with intercepts such that a+b = 2,3,…,n+1. But we have only n lines total. So we need to choose a set of n lines such that the union covers all points. The problem asks: given n, which k (number of sunny lines) can be achieved? Since sunny lines are those not parallel to the axes or slope -1. So we can have some special lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) and some sunny lines. The question is: for which k does there exist a configuration with exactly k sunny lines? Possibly all k from 0 to n are achievable, or some are not. Let's test small n manually.

We can use lines: x=1 (vertical), y=1 (horizontal), and maybe line with slope -1 passing through (1,3) and (2,2) and (3,1)? Actually line x+y=4 (slope -1) passes through (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). (2,2) on x+y=4. (3,1) on y=1 and x+y=4. So all covered. So with n=3, we can have 3 lines: one vertical (x=1), one horizontal (y=1), one slope -1 (x+y=4). Among these, which are sunny? Actually with three lines (x=1,y=1,x+y=4) we covered all points. If we try to have only two special lines, say x=1 and y=1, they cover (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). We need to cover all points. We can choose any set of three lines. Let's examine possibilities for n=3 to see which k are achievable.

Better approach: The problem is to determine all k (nonnegative integers) for which there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points. So we need to find the set of possible k values. Let's denote n≥3. We need to analyze constraints.

Observation: Each line that is not sunny (i.e., is horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) is of a special direction. Since there are only three special directions, we can think of the points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. The lines of these special directions that cover many points are: horizontal lines y = c (c=1,…,n), vertical lines x = c (c=1,…,n), and lines with slope -1 (x+y = c) for c=2,…,n+1. However note that the points with a,b positive integers and a+b≤n+1. For horizontal line y = c, it contains points (a,c) with a≥1 and a+c ≤ n+1 → a ≤ n+1-c. So it covers at most n+1-c points. Similarly vertical x = c covers at most n+1-c points. The slope -1 line x+y = c covers points (a, c-a) with a from max(1, c-n-1?) Actually a≥1, b≥1 → a≥1, c-a ≥ 1 → a ≤ c-1. Also a + (c-a) = c, and a ≤ n+1? Actually the constraint a+b ≤ n+1 is automatically satisfied if a+b = c ≤ n+1. For c ≤ n+1, all points with a+b = c and a,b≥1 are within the triangle. So the slope -1 line with intercept c (c from 2 to n+1) covers exactly c-1 points (since a from 1 to c-1, b = c-a). So it covers (c-1) points.

Now we have n lines total. We can choose any lines. The goal is to cover all points. The number of sunny lines is k. So we have n-k non-sunny lines. Since there are only three special directions, we can have multiple lines of the same direction (but distinct lines). For example, we can have several horizontal lines (different y-intercepts) covering different rows. Similarly several vertical lines, several slope -1 lines. But all non-sunny lines are among those three families. So we can choose any combination of these lines.

So with 3 lines, the maximum total points covered is 3*2 = 6, but if we use some non-sunny lines they can cover 3 points each. So for k=3 (all sunny), we need to cover 6 points with 3 lines, each covering at most 2 points, so we would need each line to cover exactly 2 points, and the union to be all points. Is it possible to partition the 6 points into 3 pairs such that each pair is collinear on a line that is sunny (i.e., not horizontal/vertical/slope -1) and the lines are distinct? And also ensure that the lines are distinct. The pairs could be: (1,1)-(2,2) (slope 1, sunny). (1,2)-(2,1) (slope -1, not sunny). (1,3)-(3,1) (slope -1, not sunny). So not all sunny. The only sunny pair is (1,1)-(2,2). (2,2)-(3,1) is slope -1. (1,3)-(2,2) slope -1. (1,2)-(2,2) slope? (1,2) to (2,2) is horizontal. (2,1)-(2,2) vertical. So indeed the only pair that is collinear with a line that is not special is (1,1)-(2,2). So we cannot cover all points with 3 sunny lines. So k=3 is impossible for n=3.

Thus for n=3, possible k are 0 and 1. Let's also consider k=2? We tried some constructions and suspect impossible. But we should check if there exists a set of 3 lines with exactly 2 sunny lines covering all points. That would require 1 non-sunny line covering some points, and 2 sunny lines covering the rest. Since total points 6, the non-sunny line could cover up to 3 points. So the two sunny lines must cover at most 3 points (if the non-sunny covers 3). Actually we need to cover all points. Suppose we use a non-sunny line that covers 3 points. Then the remaining 3 points must be covered by the two sunny lines. But as we saw, the remaining 3 points might be such that they cannot be covered by two sunny lines. However it's possible that the non-sunny line is something like y = 2 (horizontal) covering (1,2),(2,2) only (2 points) or x = 2 covering (2,1),(2,2) (2 points) or x+y=3 covering (1,2),(2,1) (2 points). So there are non-sunny lines covering 2 points. Then the two sunny lines would need to cover the remaining 4 points. But we have only 3 lines total, so if we have 2 sunny lines, they could cover at most 4 points (if each covers 2). So it's plausible. But we need to check existence.

Let's attempt a systematic construction for n=3, k=2. We need 3 lines, with exactly 2 sunny. We can choose the non-sunny line to be, say, horizontal y=1 covering (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) (3 points). Then the two sunny lines must cover (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). As we argued, those three points cannot be covered by two sunny lines because any line covering (1,2) and (1,3) is vertical (x=1) not sunny. So we need to cover them with two lines, each sunny. Could we have one sunny line covering (1,2) and (2,2), and another sunny line covering (1,3) (and maybe also (2,2) but that's okay)? But as we saw, a line covering (1,2) and (2,2) is horizontal (slope 0) not sunny. So not allowed. Could we have a sunny line covering (1,3) and (2,2) (slope -1) not allowed. So we cannot cover all three points with two sunny lines. But maybe we could cover (1,2) with one sunny line, (1,3) with another sunny line, and (2,2) with either of them (if they happen to pass through (2,2) as well). But can a sunny line pass through (1,2) and (2,2) simultaneously? No, because they are horizontal collinear; any line through both is horizontal, not sunny. So at most one of those two points can be covered by a sunny line if that line also passes through that point, but to cover (2,2) we could have a sunny line passing through (2,2) and maybe (1,3) is slope -1, not sunny; through (2,2) and (1,1) is slope 1 sunny. That covers (1,1) and (2,2). But (1,1) is already covered by the horizontal line. So that's fine. So we could have sunny line L1: y = x covering (1,1) and (2,2). That covers (2,2). Then the remaining points (1,2) and (1,3) need to be covered. They are vertical column. Can we cover them with a single sunny line? No, because any line covering both is vertical (x=1) not sunny. So we need two sunny lines to cover them. But we only have two sunny lines total, one used for L1, the other could cover (1,2) and (1,3) if it's vertical, not allowed. So maybe we could cover (1,2) with one sunny line and (1,3) with another sunny line, but that would be two sunny lines, making total sunny lines 3 (L1 plus two others), but we need exactly 2 sunny lines. So impossible.

Thus k=2 for n=3 seems impossible.

But maybe we can choose a different non-sunny line that covers, say, 2 points, and then two sunny lines covering the remaining 4 points. Let's try to see if such a configuration exists. Thus k=2 impossible for n=3.

So far, for n=3, possible k are 0 and 1. That suggests that the answer might be that k can be any integer from 0 to n, but with some restrictions. However n=3 is small, and we saw k=1 possible, k=2 impossible. So maybe for larger n, the range expands. Let's test n=4.

We need to determine all k (0 ≤ k ≤ 4) such that there exist 4 distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 5.

Points: s=2..5. For each s, points count s-1. So total points = 1+2+3+4 = 10. That's 10 points. We have 4 lines. We need to cover all 10 points.

We can try to construct examples for various k.

First, note that non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) can cover many points. For n=4, the three special directions are: horizontal lines y=c (c=1..4), vertical lines x=c (c=1..4), and slope -1 lines x+y=c (c=2..5). The maximum number of points on a horizontal line is at most (n+1-c) but for c=1, it's 4 points (since a from 1 to 4, b=1, a+b≤5 → a≤4). For c=2, 3 points; c=3, 2 points; c=4, 1 point. Similarly vertical: x=1 → 4 points; x=2 → 3 points; x=3 → 2 points; x=4 → 1 point. Slope -1: x+y=2 → 1 point (1,1); x+y=3 → 2 points (1,2),(2,1); x+y=4 → 3 points (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); x+y=5 → 4 points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So indeed each of the three families can have lines covering up to n points (for n=4, up to 4 points). The problem says "exactly k of the n lines are sunny". So we have n lines total, some are sunny, some are not. We can choose any lines. So we need to see which k are achievable.

We suspect that maybe all k from 0 to n are possible, but the n=3 case contradicts that (k=2,3 not possible). So perhaps the achievable k are those with 0 ≤ k ≤ n and k ≤ something like floor((n-1)/2) or something. Let's explore.

Observation: Non-sunny lines are those parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So they are of three families. Each such line, when passing through lattice points, can cover many points.
