First, list all points P = {(a,b)∈ℕ^+×ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ 5}. I'll list them:

s=2: (1,1)
s=3: (1,2),(2,1)
s=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)
s=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)

Total 10 points.

Now for a line L (non-sunny), we consider the set of points it contains. We'll compute union size.

Case 1: Two horizontals y = c1, c2. Since horizontals are rows. For n=4, possible rows: y=1 (4 points), y=2 (3 points), y=3 (2 points), y=4 (1 point). If we pick two horizontals, say y=1 and y=2, union size = 4+3 = 7, complement 3. If y=1 and y=3, union = 4+2 = 6, complement 4. If y=1 and y=4, union = 4+1 = 5, complement 5. If y=2 and y=3, union = 3+2 = 5, complement 5. If y=2 and y=4, union = 3+1 = 4, complement 6. If y=3 and y=4, union = 2+1 = 3, complement 7. So best is complement size 3 (y=1,y=2). That's the smallest complement. So with two horizontals, we can achieve complement size 3. Similarly two verticals: x=1 (4), x=2 (3) → union 7, complement 3. x=1 and x=3 → union 4+2=6, complement 4. etc. Best complement size 3. For two slope -1 lines: possible intercepts c from 2 to 5. c=2 → 1 point (1,1). c=3 → 2 points. c=4 → 3 points. c=5 → 4 points. So pairs like c=4 and c=5 give union 3+4 = 7, complement 3. c=3 and c=5 give 2+4 = 6, complement 4. c=3 and c=4 give 2+3 = 5, complement 5. So best complement 3. So with two non-sunny lines, we can achieve complement size 3. The smallest complement is 3. So any pair of non-sunny lines can be selected to have complement size 3 (by picking adjacent lines). That complement is a set of 3 points. For n=4, the complement with y=1,y=2 is: points not on y=1 or y=2. Which are those? Let's list all points and remove those with y=1 or y=2. Points with y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). Points with y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). That's 7 points. Missing points: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,1?) Wait (3,1) is y=1, covered. So missing: (1,3) y=3, (1,4) y=4, (2,3) y=3, (3,? Actually (3,2) covered, (4,1) covered. So missing: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3). Also (3,? But (3,? ) there is (3,1) covered, (3,2) covered. So indeed missing (1,3),(1,4),(2,3). That's 3 points. Similarly with x=1,x=2 gives missing (3,1),(2,2),(4,1) maybe? Let's compute. But anyway.

Now the complement size is 3. Can we cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines? Yes, if those 3 points are collinear on a sunny line, then one sunny line covers all, and the second sunny line can be anything else (maybe redundant). But are there any three points in the triangle that lie on a sunny line? For n=4, the complement points from y=1,y=2 are (1,3),(1,4),(2,3). Are they collinear? Check slope (4-3)/(1-2) = 1/(-1) = -1. That's slope -1, not sunny. So they are not on a sunny line. Could they be covered by two sunny lines such that each covers two of them? But as argued, any two of them are either vertical (x=1) or horizontal (y=3) or slope -1. So no sunny line can contain two of them. So each sunny line can cover at most 1 of these 3 points. With 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points covered, leaving one uncovered. So impossible.

Thus for n=4, k=2 impossible.

Now k=3 for n=4? That would be 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny. We have 4 lines total. The non-sunny line covers at most 4 points. Sunny lines cover at most 2 each, total at most 6. But we need to cover 10 points. So obviously impossible because the maximum union size is at most (max points covered by non-sunny) + (max points covered by sunny) but note that overlapping may reduce union, but the sum of maximums is 4+6=10, which equals |P|. So it's not impossible by that bound alone; but we need to check if we can actually cover all points with 1 non-sunny and 3 sunny lines. The non-sunny line could be, say, x+y=5 covering 4 points. Then we have 3 sunny lines covering at most 2 each, total 6. That would cover up to 10 points if the sunny lines cover disjoint sets not already covered. However we have 10 points total. So it's possible in principle if we can arrange that the sunny lines cover the remaining 6 points (the complement of the non-sunny line). The complement after taking x+y=5 is points with a+b ≤ 4, i.e., (1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 6 points. So we need to cover those 6 points with 3 sunny lines. As we argued, each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from this triangle. So 3 sunny lines could cover up to 6 points. So it's plausible. But we need to see if those 6 points can be partitioned into 3 pairs such that each pair is collinear on a sunny line. The points are: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Let's examine collinearity among them. We need to find three sunny lines (i.e., lines with slopes not 0,∞,-1) that each pass through two of these points (or possibly through more). Since we have 6 points, we could try to cover them with three lines, each covering two points. However are there any lines that are sunny covering two points from this set? Let's compute slopes:

Pairs:
(1,1)-(1,2): vertical (x=1) not sunny.

(1,1)-(1,3): vertical.

(2,2)-(3,1): slope -1.

The three families are limited. Each line can cover at most n points. So the maximum union size from m lines is at most (max points per line) * m, but due to overlaps, it's less. So the total coverage from non-sunny lines plus sunny lines must be all points. The non-sunny lines are special; they can cover many points. So we need to see for which n we can achieve k=2. Possibly for n large enough, we can choose n-2 non-sunny lines that cover almost all points, leaving a small set that can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Let's analyze.

General n≥3. The points P = {(a,b)∈ℕ^+×ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}. Total number of points = 1+2+...+n = n(n+1)/2.

We have n lines. Let T be the set of non-sunny lines, |T| = n - k. Each non-sunny line is either horizontal (y = constant), vertical (x = constant), or slope -1 (x+y = constant). We can choose which constants. Sunny lines are arbitrary.

The union of T covers some set of points. The complement U = P \ (union of T) is the set of points that need to be covered by sunny lines. Let s = |U|. Then we need s ≤ 2k, because each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from P (since any line with slope not 0,∞,-1 can contain at most 2 points of P). However is it possible that a sunny line contains more than 2 points from P if its slope is something like 1/2 and it passes through many lattice points within the triangle? Let's analyze more carefully.

Given P is a set of integer lattice points within a right triangle. Consider a line L (in the plane). The intersection L ∩ P is a set of integer points. The number of integer points on a line can be arbitrarily large if the line has rational slope and passes through many lattice points. But here P is bounded and has a specific shape. Could there be a line with slope, say, 1/2 that contains 3 points of P? Let's examine. For n large, P includes many points. Suppose we have a line y = (1/2)x + b. For integer x, y is integer only when x is even. So such a line can contain points with x even. It could contain multiple points within the triangle if the triangle is large. For example, take n=10, P includes points up to a+b ≤ 11. The line y = (1/2)x + 1 could pass through (2,2), (4,3), (6,4), (8,5), (10,6) perhaps, but need to check if those satisfy a+b ≤ 11? (2,2) sum 4 ≤11, (4,3) sum 7, (6,4) sum 10, (8,5) sum 13 >11, so not all. So maybe at most 2 points? But need a rigorous bound.

Observation: For any line L that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, the set of integer points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 that lie on L is at most 2. Is this always true? Let's test with n=5, we saw that the line y = 2x (slope 2) passes through (1,2) and (2,4) (since 2+4=6 ≤6). That's 2 points. Could there be a line with slope 1/2 that passes through (2,2) and (4,3) and (6,4) but (6,4) sum 10 ≤ n+1? For n=5, n+1=6, so (6,4) sum 10 >6, so not in P. For n=10, n+1=11, (10,6) sum 16 >11, so not. So maybe indeed the maximum is 2. But is it possible to have a line with slope, say, 3/2 that passes through (2,3) and (4,6) (sum 10) and (6,9) (sum 15) etc. The triangle restricts a+b ≤ n+1. So the line can intersect the triangle in a segment; the integer points on that line within the triangle are those with a+b ≤ n+1. For a line that is not one of the three special directions, the set of integer points on it within this triangle can be at most 2. Let's try to prove this.

We have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. Suppose a line L (not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1) contains three distinct points P1,P2,P3 from this set. Since L is not vertical, we can param. The points are integer lattice points. The slope of L is rational. We can consider the differences. But perhaps there is a more combinatorial argument: The lines of special directions partition the triangle in some way. However we need to be careful. Let's attempt to prove:

Claim: For n≥3, any line L (in the plane) that is not horizontal, not vertical, and not of slope -1 can contain at most 2 points from the set S_n = {(a,b)∈ℕ^+×ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}.

Proof approach: Suppose L contains three points A,B,C ∈ S_n. Then the slope of L is (b2-b1)/(a2-a1) which is rational. The three points are collinear. Consider the lines L and the three special directions. Since L is not parallel to any of those, its slope is different from 0, ∞, -1. We want to derive a contradiction using the bound a+b ≤ n+1. Let's attempt to show that if a line contains three integer points with a+b ≤ n+1, then its slope must be 0, ∞, or -1, or the points are not all within the triangle? That seems plausible because the triangle is "thin". Let's examine.

Take three collinear integer points (a1,b1),(a2,b2),(a3,b3). Consider the vector differences. If the line is not one of the special directions, then the differences have some relation. The condition a+b ≤ n+1 imposes a linear inequality. Perhaps we can consider the projection onto the direction (1,1). The quantity a+b is linear. For a line with slope ≠ -1, the values of a+b along the line are not constant; they change. If the line contains three points, then the a+b values at those points are three distinct numbers (or maybe some equal). But if the line is not parallel to (1,1) direction, then a+b is strictly monotonic along the line. So the three a+b values are distinct. The maximum a+b is ≤ n+1. That's okay.

But why would that limit the number of integer points? There's no obvious bound. For example, consider the line y = 2x. It contains points (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) etc. For n=6, (3,6) sum=9 ≤7? Actually n+1=7, so (3,6) sum=9 >7, so not in S_6. For n=7, n+1=8, (3,6) sum=9 >8, so not. So maybe the triangle's shape prevents three points on a line with slope not special. But is there a line with slope, say, 2/3 that passes through (3,2) and (6,4) and (9,6)? Sums: (3,2)=5, (6,4)=10, (9,6)=15. So if n is large enough (n≥14, n+1≥15), then (9,6) is in S_n. So with n=14, S_14 includes points up to a+b ≤ 15. (9,6) sum=15 ≤15. So we have three points on line y = (2/3)x? But (3,2) and (6,4) and (9,6) are collinear with slope 2/3? Let's compute slope: (4-2)/(6-3) = 2/3. (6-4)/(9-6) = 2/3. So yes. So for n=14, we have three points (3,2),(6,4),(9,6) all in S_14? Check (3,2): 3+2=5 ≤15, ok. (6,4): 6+4=10 ≤15, ok. (9,6): 9+6=15 ≤15, ok. So indeed we have three collinear points on a line with slope 2/3, which is not 0, ∞, -1. So the claim that any line not special can contain at most 2 points from S_n is false for large n. So we need to reconsider.

But note that the problem says "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0." So sunny lines are those with slopes not 0, ∞, -1. But they could be any other slope, possibly rational slopes like 2/3, 1/2, etc. And as we just saw, such a line can contain many lattice points if it passes through many integer points. For S_n (the triangle of points), the maximum number of collinear points with slope not 0,∞,-1 can be more than 2, depending on n. For example, for n=14, we found three points on line y = (2/3)x (but we need to check that the line passes through (3,2),(6,4),(9,6) indeed; but does it also pass through (12,8) etc? That would be further). So the bound is not trivial. However, note that in the problem, we are allowed to choose any lines (including those that are not special). The sunny lines are those not parallel to the axes or x+y=0. So they can have any other slope. So we need to analyze the covering problem with that in mind.

But we earlier derived that with n=3,4,5, k=2 seemed impossible, and we argued that the complement after using some non-sunny lines had 3 points, and any sunny line could cover at most 1 of them because any pair among those points is collinear on a non-sunny direction. That argument used that the complement points (like (2,2),(2,3),(3,2)) are such that any line containing two of them is parallel to one of the three special directions. That's true. So for n=5, those three points are all in S_5. And indeed any sunny line (slope ≠ 0,∞,-1) cannot contain two of them. So with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 can be covered. So k=2 impossible. For n=6, we considered a set of 4 non-sunny lines that left complement of size 3 (points with a,b≥2 and a+b ≤ 5: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2)). Those are exactly the same as in n=5, but note that for n=6, the triangle includes points up to a+b≤7, so (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) are still present. So complement size 3. That's still problematic. But we have 2 sunny lines. Could we design a different set of non-sunny lines (size n-2 = 4) such that the complement is of size ≤ 4? The complement size after using m non-sunny lines is at least something. Let's analyze more systematically.

We have n lines total. Let m = n - k be the number of non-sunny lines. Then we have k sunny lines. We need to cover all P points. We can choose which specific lines. The sunny lines can be any lines; they can cover many points if they have rational slopes that pass through many lattice points. So we cannot simply bound each sunny line by 2. That bound is only for lines that are not special, but we are using sunny lines (which are not special), so they could contain many points. However, in our earlier argument for n=5, we used that any sunny line can contain at most 2 points from the complement (the set of 3 points) because any line containing two of those points is non-sunny. But we need to consider that the sunny lines themselves might be chosen to be lines that contain multiple points from P, not necessarily from the complement. But we are covering all points. The complement is the set of points not covered by non-sunny lines. So we need to cover that complement with sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover any points (including those already covered by non-sunny lines). The question is: can we find k sunny lines such that their union (together with the non-sunny lines) covers all points. So if complement size is 3, we could try to cover those 3 points with, say, 2 sunny lines, but as argued, each sunny line can contain at most 1 of those 3 points. So 2 sunny lines can cover at most 2. So impossible. Thus for k=2 to be possible, we need that after selecting n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement U has size ≤ 2 (or at most 2k, but we also need that they can be covered by k sunny lines). But more importantly, we need that there exists a set of n-2 non-sunny lines such that the complement U can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Since sunny lines can be arbitrary, we can also choose them to cover points that are not in U (if U is small, we can cover them with some sunny lines). But we need to cover all points. So if U is small, say size 2, we could cover them with 2 sunny lines (each covering one point) or even one sunny line covering both if they are collinear on a sunny line. But we must also ensure that the non-sunny lines we choose are distinct. The problem asks to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions. So we can attempt to construct configurations for various k. The difficulty is to determine which k are possible. Based on small n, k=0 and 1 are always possible. k=2 seems impossible for n=3,4,5. What about n=6? Let's try to see if we can construct for k=2 with n=6 lines. We need to cover 21 points with 6 lines, exactly 2 of which are sunny. So we have 4 non-sunny lines. We need to find 4 non-sunny lines that cover at least 17 points, leaving at most 4 uncovered, which then can be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we also need that those 4 uncovered points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. That seems plausible if the uncovered set is, say, 4 points that are arranged in two pairs each collinear on a sunny line. But we need to ensure that the uncovered set does not contain a triple of points that are all on non-sunny lines (like the 3 points we saw). Actually the uncovered set is the complement of the union of the non-sunny lines. We can choose the non-sunny lines to cover many points, perhaps all but 2 or 3. But the complement might be larger. Let's compute the maximum possible union size from m non-sunny lines. For n=6, we have 21 points. With m=4, can we cover all 21 points? Possibly, if we choose the 4 lines appropriately. But the non-sunny lines are limited to special directions. The maximum union size from m such lines is something like: we can take m lines that are far apart in each family. However, there are only 3 families. To cover all points, we might need many lines. The triangle's points are arranged in a grid; the special lines (horizontals, verticals, slope -1) can cover all points if we take enough of them. For example, we could take all horizontals y=1..n (n lines) which would cover all points. Similarly all verticals. So with m = n (all lines non-sunny), we can cover all points. But we have n lines total; if we set k = 0, we use n non-sunny lines, which works. For k = 1, we use n-1 non-sunny lines. That seems to work as we saw for n=3,4,5. For k = 2, we use n-2 non-sunny lines. So we need to check whether we can cover all points with n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are special directions; we can choose which ones. The question reduces to: what's the minimum number of special-direction lines required to cover all points in P? If that minimum is ≤ n-2, then we can set k = 2 (or even larger). But if the minimum is larger than n-2, then k = 2 is impossible. Actually we need exactly k sunny lines; we can also use sunny lines to cover some points, but if we want to minimize the number of sunny lines, we can use more non-sunny lines (which are special). The maximum number of non-sunny lines we can have is n (k=0). So we can try to cover all points with n-2 non-sunny lines; if that's impossible, maybe we can cover with n-2 non-sunny lines plus 2 sunny lines. But if the complement after using n-2 non-sunny lines is too large (more than 2k), then even with k sunny lines we cannot cover them (since each sunny line can cover many points). However sunny lines could cover many points, so they are not limited to 2. So we need a more careful analysis.

Let's re-evaluate. The bound of 2 per sunny line was based on the assumption that a line with slope not 0,∞,-1 can contain at most 2 points from the triangle. But as we saw, for large n, there exist lines with rational slopes (e.g., slope 2/3) that contain many integer points within the triangle. So that bound is false. Therefore we cannot simply argue that each sunny line covers at most 2 points. Instead, we need to consider the covering problem with the possibility that sunny lines can cover many points. So the earlier reasoning for n=5 that k=2 is impossible relied on the fact that after using 3 non-sunny lines, the complement was a set of 3 points, and any sunny line can contain at most 1 of those points because any line containing two of those points would be non-sunny. That argument is valid regardless of whether sunny lines can contain many points in general; it's about those specific points. But we need to consider that the complement might be covered by sunny lines that also contain other points, but we only need to cover the complement. Thus for m = n - 2, the maximum union size is 3 less than total points. So complement size is 3. That seems robust.

Now we need to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines covering P. Since we have n lines total, and we can decide how many are sunny. For each k, we can try to construct a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines. If k is large, we need many sunny lines, but then we have fewer non-sunny lines. The complement after using non-sunny lines may be large, and we need to cover it with sunny lines. The sunny lines can be chosen to cover many points. But we need to check the feasibility. The problem likely expects a solution that determines the set of possible k in terms of n, probably something like: all k such that k ≤ n - 2 or k = n? But we saw n=3, k=1 (which is n-2 = 1) works, n=4, k=3 (n-1) works, but k=2 (n-2) fails. So n-2 is not always achievable. For n=3, n-2=1 works. For n=4, n-2=2 fails. For n=5, n-2=3 works? Actually n-2=3, we saw k=3 works. So n-2 works for n=3,5 but not for n=4. So pattern maybe related to parity of n? Let's test n=4: n-2=2 fails. n=5: n-2=3 works. n=6: n-2=4 fails? We haven't determined k=4 for n=6. That's k = n-2 = 4. We need to see if it's possible with 6 lines, 4 sunny, 2 non-sunny. That's the opposite: if we want k = n-2, we have m = 2 non-sunny lines. With 2 non-sunny lines, the best we can do is to take the largest two lines from a family, which cover sizes n + (n-1) = 2n-1 points? Actually for n=6, the two largest horizontals are y=1 (6), y=2 (5) => sum 11, union size 11. That leaves complement 10. Then we have 4 sunny lines. Those 4 sunny lines need to cover 10 points. That's plausible. So maybe k = n-2 is achievable for n=6 as well? But earlier we argued that with m = n-2 (non-sunny) we have complement size 3, but that's for k=2. For k = n-2, we have m = 2, which is much smaller. So the number of sunny lines is larger, but we have more flexibility. So we need to consider all k from 0 to n.

Let's approach systematically: We have n lines. Let a = n - k be the number of non-sunny lines. Then we have k sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are from the three families. The sunny lines can be any lines (including those that might be parallel to special directions, but they are not allowed because they would be non-sunny). So sunny lines are not parallel to axes or slope -1.

We need to cover all P points. We can choose which specific lines. The question is: for which k does there exist a set of n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines satisfying covering? So we can attempt to construct for various k. The problem likely expects an answer like: k can be any integer from 0 to n except when k is even and greater than 1? But we need to test more values, maybe all k are possible except when k is even and k ≤ n-2? That seems messy.

Better approach: We can attempt to construct examples for all k from 0 to n, then prove impossibility for some k. The small n experiments show that for n=3, k=2,3 impossible; for n=4, k=2 impossible, but k=3 possible; for n=5, k=2 impossible, k=3 possible; what about k=4 for n=5? That's k = n-1, which is 4. That's a lot of sunny lines. With 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny, can we cover all points? The non-sunny line can be, say, x=1 (covers 5 points). The complement is 10 points. Then we have 4 sunny lines. Are 4 sunny lines enough to cover 10 points? Possibly yes. But we need to check if there is any obstruction. However we should also consider the possibility that some k are impossible because the number of sunny lines is insufficient to cover the uncovered points after using the maximum possible non-sunny lines. But we can also use sunny lines that are not necessarily covering the complement only; they can also cover some points that are already covered, but that doesn't matter. The question is: given n lines, can we cover all P? The lines are distinct. We can choose any set. So we can try to design a configuration where the non-sunny lines are as many as possible (k=0) and then for larger k, we replace some non-sunny lines with sunny lines. But we must maintain covering property. So we can try to see if we can always achieve k=1 (which we already did for n=3,4,5). That's easy. For k=2, we need to see if it's possible for any n≥3. Let's test n=3, impossible; n=4, impossible; n=5, impossible; n=6, likely impossible. So maybe k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need to confirm with a proof. Let's attempt to prove that if k=2, then n lines with exactly 2 sunny lines cannot cover all P. That is, we need to show that with n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement (points not on those lines) has size at least 3, and those points are such that any line containing two of them is non-sunny, so they cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we also have the possibility that the 2 sunny lines are not necessarily covering only the complement; they could cover some points that are also covered by non-sunny lines. The complement is the set of points not covered by the non-sunny lines. To cover all points, we need to cover that complement with the sunny lines. Since each sunny line can contain at most 1 of those points (as argued), we need at least |U| sunny lines. So if |U| ≥ 3, then k ≥ 3. Therefore, if we can prove that for any selection of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement U has size at least 3, then k=2 is impossible. But is it always true that the complement size is at least 3? The maximum union size from n-2 non-sunny lines is at most something, and we can try to bound the complement size from below. However we must consider that the non-sunny lines are not arbitrary; they can be any lines of special directions. The maximum union size achievable with n-2 such lines is something like H_{n-2} = (n-2)(n+3)/2, which is less than n(n+1)/2 by 3. That gives a lower bound on complement size: C ≥ 3. But is it possible that the union size is larger than H_{n-2}? For example, if we take some lines from different families, could the union be larger than the sum of the n-2 largest lines from a single family? The sum of the n-2 largest lines from a family is H_{n-2}. However, we might also take lines that are not the largest in size, but some lines from other families that are larger in size than some of the largest lines in that family. But note that the largest line in any family is size n, the next is n-1, etc. So the sum of the n-2 largest lines in a family is n + (n-1) + ... + (n - (n-2) + 1) = n + (n-1) + ... + 3. That's (n-2)(n+3)/2. That's a specific number. Could we get a larger union by mixing? For instance, if we take all n-2 lines from one family, that's H_{n-2}. If we take some from other families, we might cover some points that are not covered by those lines, but we also might cover fewer points due to overlaps. The union size cannot exceed the total number of points. The complement size is total minus union. The minimal possible complement size is achieved when union is maximal. So we need to find the maximum possible union size with n-2 non-sunny lines. If that maximum is something like (n-2)(n+3)/2, then the complement size is at least 3. But if there is a combination that yields a larger union, say (n-2)(n+3)/2 + something, then complement could be smaller. But can we achieve union size = n(n+1)/2 - 2 or 1? That would require covering almost all points. Let's see for n=6, we found union 18 with 4 lines. That's total 21 - 3. Could we get 19? That would be complement 2. Is it possible with 4 lines? Let's try to cover all points except two. For n=6, total points 21. If we take lines like y=1, y=2, y=3, y=4, that's 4 lines covering 6+5+4+3 = 18. Complement 3. If we try to take y=1, y=2, x=1, x=2, we got union 18 (complement 3). If we take y=1, y=2, y=3, y=4? That's 4 lines, union 6+5+4+3 = 18. That's the same. Could we take y=1, y=2, y=3, x=1? That's 4 lines: y1,y2,y3 sizes 6+5+4=15, plus x1 size 6, overlaps at (1,1) (y1 and x1), (1,2) (y2 and x1), (1,3) (y3 and x1) and maybe (1,? Actually (1,1) y1 and x1, (1,2) y2 and x1, (1,3) y3 and x1. That's 3 overlaps. So union = 15+6 - 3 = 18. Same. So seems the maximum union is 18. Could we get 19 by picking lines like y=1, y=2, y=3, and then some line of slope -1 that covers some new points but also overlaps? But we already used 3 horizontals covering rows 1-3 (sizes 6+5+4 = 15). The complement after those is points with y≥4. That's (1,4),(2,4),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) etc. Actually for n=6, the points with y≥4 are: y=4: (1,4),(2,4),(3,4) → 3 points; y=5: (1,5),(2,5) → 2 points; y=6: (1,6) → 1 point; total 6 points. Adding a line like x=1 would cover some of those, but we already have x=1 is not used. However we have 4 lines; if we use y=1,y=2,y=3 (3 lines) and then another line (maybe slope -1) that covers some new points, but note that the union size after 3 horizontals is 15, complement 6. Adding a line of a special direction can cover at most something. The sum of sizes of that line is at most 6. However overlaps could be large. For example, adding y=4 (size 3) would add 3 new points (since rows 1-3 cover y=1,2,3, and y=4 is disjoint), union becomes 18, complement 3. That's exactly the 4 horizontals case. If we add a line like x=1 (size 6), overlaps with the horizontals: y=1 and x=1 intersect at (1,1); y=2 and x=1 at (1,2); y=3 and x=1 at (1,3). That's 3 overlaps, so union = 15+6-3 = 18. So still 18. Could we add a line of slope -1, like x+y=7 (size 6) that intersects each of the horizontals at points: y=1 ∩ x+y=7 → (6,1) (since 6+1=7), y=2 ∩ x+y=7 → (5,2), y=3 ∩ x+y=7 → (4,3). That's 3 overlaps. So union = 15+6-3 = 18. So indeed any 4th line gives union 18. So maximum is 18. So complement size 3. So for n=6, k=2 impossible. Similarly for n=7, m=5, union with 5 horizontals (y=1..5) sizes 7+6+5+4+3 = 25, total points 28, complement 3. So pattern holds: with m = n-2, maximum union is n(n+1)/2 - 3. So complement size at least 3. Therefore, with exactly k=2 sunny lines, we have m = n-2 non-sunny lines. The complement U has size at least 3. But we need to cover U with 2 sunny lines. As argued, any two points in U are collinear on a non-sunny line (we need to prove that). However is it always the case that U, after selecting m = n-2 non-sunny lines, will be a set of 3 points (or more) such that any line containing two of them is non-sunny? Possibly U could be larger, but still we need to cover it with 2 sunny lines. The obstacle is that each sunny line can cover at most 1 of those points, because if it covered two, then those two points would be collinear on a sunny line, but we need to examine whether that can happen with points in U. However note that U is the complement of the union of the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are of special directions. The points in U are those that are not on any of those lines. Could it be that two points in U happen to lie on a line that is sunny (i.e., not parallel to axes or slope -1)? Possibly, if those points are not aligned on a special direction. But we need to prove that for any selection of n-2 non-sunny lines, the set U (size ≥ 3) has the property that any two points in U are collinear on a line that is not sunny? Actually we need to show that there is no sunny line that contains two points of U. But is that always true? Let's analyze.

We have n-2 non-sunny lines. They are chosen from the families H, V, S (slope -1). The union of these lines covers some points. The complement U is the set of points not covered. Could U contain two points that lie on a sunny line? Suppose we have two points P and Q in U. They are not on any of the chosen non-sunny lines. Could they be collinear with a line that is not special (sunny)? If so, that line would be a candidate sunny line covering both. That would be okay; we could use that sunny line to cover both. So the issue is not that any two points in U cannot be on a sunny line, but that we need to cover all points in U with only k sunny lines. For k=2, we have 2 sunny lines. If U has size 3, then we need to cover 3 points with 2 lines. Even if two of those points can be covered by one sunny line, and the third by another, that would be 2 lines covering 3 points. That's possible if there exists a sunny line covering two of them. But we need to see whether such a configuration exists with the non-sunny lines chosen appropriately. So the impossibility claim for k=2 is not automatically that U has size ≥ 3 and that any line containing two of U is non-sunny; we need to prove that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement U (size at least 3) is such that no sunny line can contain two points of U. But is it possible to choose non-sunny lines that leave a U of size 3 where two of those points are collinear on a sunny line? Let's examine the structure of P.

We have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1. The non-sunny lines are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The points that are not covered by a particular set of such lines might be located in a certain region. For example, if we take the n-2 largest horizontals, U consists of points with y≥n-1 (since y=1..n-2 are covered). That's a small set. Let's compute U for n=6, m=4, using y=1,2,3,4 (horizontals). U = {(1,5),(2,5),(1,6)}. Are any two of these on a sunny line? (1,5)-(2,5) is horizontal, not sunny. (1,5)-(1,6) vertical, not sunny. (2,5)-(1,6) slope -1, not sunny. So indeed no sunny line covers two. That's because U is a set of points that are all in the "upper right" region and are arranged such that any two are either vertical, horizontal, or slope -1. This seems to be a general phenomenon: The points that are not covered by the largest m horizontals (y=1..m) are those with y > m. But note that the triangle's shape: For y > m, the x-coordinate is limited. Those points are such that any two of them are either on a horizontal (if same y) or vertical (if same x) or slope -1? Let's analyze.

Suppose we take the m largest horizontals. Then the complement U is the set of points with y ≥ m+1. These points have coordinates (a,b) with b ≥ m+1, a ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1. For such points, the differences in a and b are limited. The set of points with b ≥ m+1 is a small triangle at the top. In such a set, any two points are either with different b and a. Let's try to find two points in U that are collinear on a sunny line. For example, for n=7, m=5, U after taking y=1..5 (horizontals) would be points with y=6,7. y=6: (1,6),(2,6) sum7, (3,6) sum9>8? Actually n=7 => n+1=8, so y=6 => a≤2 (since 6+2=8). So points (1,6),(2,6). y=7: (1,7). That's three points. Are any two of these on a sunny line? (1,6)-(2,6) horizontal; (1,6)-(1,7) vertical; (2,6)-(1,7) slope (7-6)/(1-2)=1/(-1) = -1. So none sunny. So indeed not.

Now could there be a different choice of non-sunny lines (like a mix) such that U has size 3 but two of those points lie on a sunny line? For instance, take n=6, m=4. We saw that taking y=1,2,3,4 gives U = {(1,5),(2,5),(1,6)}. Could we choose 4 lines that are not all horizontals, but still cover 18 points, and leave a U of size 3 that is different, maybe with points that are not all on special lines? But the maximum union size is 18; any set of 4 special lines can cover at most 18 points, leaving at least 3 uncovered. So U has size at least 3. The structure of U depends on which lines are chosen. But we need to show that for any selection of 4 special lines, the uncovered points are such that no sunny line can contain two of them. However is it possible that U has size 3 but the three points are, say, (2,2), (2,3), (3,2) which we saw are all on non-sunny lines? But those points are covered by some special lines; if we choose non-sunny lines that cover all other points, then U might be exactly those three. For n=5, we had U = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)} with m=3. That's size 3. And indeed those three points are such that any two are on a non-sunny line. For n=6, if we take m=4, could we get U = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}? That's size 3. But is that possible? Let's test. For n=6, the points with a,b≥2 and a+b≤5 are (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) (and also (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) are the only such with a+b≤5). But there are also points with a+b=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So if we cover all points except those three, we would have union size 21-3 = 18. So we need to cover 18 points with 4 special lines. Can we cover all points except those three? That would mean that the union of the 4 lines contains all points except (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). But is that possible? Let's try to see if there is a set of 4 special lines that covers all points except those three. For n=6, the three points (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) are in the lower right region. They are not on the "border" of the triangle. Could we choose lines like y=1,2,3,4 (horizontals) covering rows 1-4, which leaves points with y≥5 (which are (1,5),(2,5),(1,6) etc). That doesn't cover (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) because those have y=2,3, so they are covered by y=2,y=3. So those are not uncovered. So to leave those three uncovered, we need to avoid covering them. But we have 4 lines; we could try to cover all points except those three. But can we cover all other points? For example, take lines: y=1, y=4, x=1, x=5? That seems unlikely. Let's systematically consider the possibilities.

We need to see whether there exists a selection of n-2 non-sunny lines that results in U being a set of points where at least two are collinear on a sunny line. But perhaps it's easier to prove that for any selection of m = n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement U has size at least 3, and moreover U is a subset of the points that are not on the "border" of the triangle, but we need a property that any line passing through two points of U is non-sunny. However, is it always true that for any selection of m special lines, U is contained in the set of points that are "far from the axes" and thus any two are on a non-sunny line? Let's think.

The complement U is the set of points not covered by the chosen special lines. Since the special lines are axis-aligned or slope -1, they cover many points. The uncovered points are those that avoid all those lines. If we choose lines that are all from one family, U is a small set at the top or right or diagonal. That set has the property that any two points are on a line that is not sunny (as we saw). But if we choose a different combination, U could be a different set. For instance, if we choose 3 lines: y=1, y=2, x=1 (3 lines) and then we need a 4th line, but we are considering m=4, so we could choose some lines that are not all from same family. The complement might be something like {(3,3),(4,2),(5,1)} maybe? But that seems unlikely because with 4 lines we can cover at most 18 points. The complement size is at least 3. The question is: is it possible that U contains three points such that two of them are not on a special direction, i.e., they lie on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1? If so, then with 2 sunny lines, we could cover those points (one sunny line covering two, the other covering the third). So we need to examine whether for n≥3, there exists a set of n-2 special lines such that the complement U has size 3 and those 3 points are not all pairwise on special lines. But we also need to cover the entire P, so U must be covered by sunny lines. If U is of size 3, we need at least 2 sunny lines, but we have exactly 2 sunny lines. So if there exists a sunny line covering two of them, then the union of that sunny line and another sunny line covering the third would cover U. So it's plausible. But is it possible with the constraints of covering? We need to also ensure that the union of the non-sunny lines covers all points except those three. That's a covering problem. Let's attempt to construct for n=6, m=4, to cover all points except (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Can we find 4 special lines that cover the other 18 points? Let's list all points P for n=6 (21 points). The points we want to leave uncovered are U0 = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}. We need to see if there exists a set T of 4 lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) such that P \ T = U0. That is, T covers all points except those three. Equivalently, U0 must be disjoint from T. So T must not contain any of those points. Also T must cover all other points. So we need to find 4 lines that avoid covering those points, but still cover all other points. Is that possible? Let's try to design.

We can try to use lines that are "far" from those points. For instance, take y=1 (covers (1,1)-(6,1)). That covers (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1) and (1,1). That's okay. It does not cover (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's fine. Next, y=2 (covers (1,2)-(5,2)). That covers (2,2) and (3,2). That's not allowed because we want to leave those uncovered. So y=2 would cover them. So we cannot use y=2. Similarly, y=3 covers (1,3)-(4,3). That includes (2,3) (since 2+3=5 ≤7). So cannot use y=3. y=4 covers (1,4)-(3,4). That includes (2,4) not in U0. That's okay. But we need to cover all points except U0. So we need to cover points like (2,4), (3,3), etc. The issue is that we cannot use some horizontals because they would cover U0. So we might use a different family. Maybe we can use some vertical lines. x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,6). That includes (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(1,6). That's fine; does not cover U0. x=2 covers (2,1)-(2,5). That includes (2,2),(2,3) -> U0 covered. So cannot use x=2. x=3 covers (3,1)-(3,4). That includes (3,2) -> U0 covered. So cannot use x=3. x=4 covers (4,1)-(4,3). That includes (4,2) not in U0, but also (4,3) maybe. That's okay. So we have constraints. We need 4 lines. Could we take y=1, y=4, x=1, and some slope -1 line like x+y=7 (covers (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1)). Does that cover all points except U0? Let's compute union.

Points: U0 = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}. We'll cover all others. But we must ensure that no point in U0 is covered. Check:

y=1 covers (1,1)-(6,1). That's fine.
y=4 covers (1,4),(2,4),(3,4). That's fine (doesn't cover U0). x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,6). That's fine.
x+y=7 covers (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1). That's fine.
Now, is every point P not in U0 covered? Let's list all points P and see which are missing. We'll compute union of these 4 lines.

But note that we used 4 lines: y=1, y=4, x=1, x+y=7. That's 4 lines. Let's compute union size.

First, y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(6,1)

Thus to achieve complement size 3, we need to select lines that cover 18 points. The maximum we found is 18 with, say, 4 horizontals (y=1-4). That covers rows 1-4, leaving rows 5-6 uncovered (3 points). That's a specific complement. Could there be another selection covering 18 points with a complement of size 3 that is not those top points? Possibly, but we need to examine the structure. The complement after selecting m special lines is the set of points that are not on any of those lines. The union size is maximized by taking lines from a single family that are as large as possible. That gives complement size = total - sum_{i=1}^{m} (n - i + 1) = n(n+1)/2 - m(2n - m + 1)/2. For m = n-2, we computed complement size 3. So any set of n-2 special lines that achieves union size 18 (for n=6) must have union size at least 18. But is it possible to have union size 18 with a different selection, not all from same family, but still covering 18 points? The sum of sizes of m lines from the same family is H_m = (n-2)(n+3)/2. For n=6, that's 4*9/2 = 18. So that's maximum. Any other combination would have either smaller sum of sizes or more overlaps, thus union size ≤ H_m. So indeed the maximum union size is H_m. Therefore, any set of n-2 special lines covers at most H_m points, leaving at least 3 uncovered. So U has size at least 3. That's a lower bound.

Now, could U have size > 3? Possibly, but then we have more uncovered points. With k=2 sunny lines, we need to cover U. If U has size 3, we need to see if it's possible to cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines. As we saw, if U is exactly the set of points with y≥m+1 after taking the largest m horizontals, then those points are such that no sunny line contains two of them. So covering requires at least 3 sunny lines. But if there exists a different selection of n-2 lines that yields U of size 3, but those 3 points are not all on special lines, maybe they can be covered by 2 sunny lines. However we need to prove that for any selection of n-2 special lines, the uncovered set U (size ≥ 3) has the property that any two points of U are collinear on a non-sunny line. Is that always true? Not obviously. Let's test with n=6, m=4. The maximum union size is 18. So any covering with 4 special lines will cover at most 18 points, leaving at least 3 uncovered. We need to see if there is any selection of 4 special lines that covers 18 points. The maximum is 18. That's achieved by taking 4 horizontals (y=1,2,3,4). Also by taking 4 verticals (x=1,2,3,4). So indeed those are the only ways to achieve maximum. But we need to consider that we might also take a combination that yields union size 18 even if not all from same family, but the sum of sizes might exceed H_m, but overlaps reduce union. To get union 18, we need the sum of sizes minus overlaps to be 18. The maximum possible sum of sizes for m=4 lines is 4*n = 24, but overlaps are significant. The maximum union size is indeed H_m. So any set of 4 special lines that covers 18 points must have union size 18, which is the maximum. But are there sets that achieve 18 that are not all from one family? For example, taking y=1 (6), y=2 (5), y=3 (4), and y=4 (3) gives 18. That's all horizontals. So indeed we can have a combination of horizontals and a vertical achieving 18. In that case, what is U? The uncovered points are those not on any of those 4 lines. Thus it seems plausible that for any selection of n-2 special lines, the complement U has size at least 3, and moreover the points in U are such that any line containing two of them is a special direction (i.e., parallel to one of the axes or slope -1). But is it always true? Let's try to prove a stronger statement: For any set T of special lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) with |T| = n-2, the set U = P \ (union of T) is a subset of the points that are "far" from the axes and such that any two points of U are collinear on a line that is not sunny. However, we need a rigorous proof that for any such T, if |U| ≥ 3, then any two points in U lie on a line that is not sunny (i.e., is parallel to one of the three special directions). But is it possible that U contains three points that are not all pairwise on special lines, but still the union of the special lines covers all other points? For example, consider n=7, m=5. The maximum union size is 25, complement 3. If we take the five largest horizontals (y=1..5), U = points with y≥6. That's {(1,6),(1,7),(2,6)}? Actually n+1=8, so y=6: a≤2 (since 6+2=8). So (1,6),(2,6). y=7: (1,7). That's 3 points. Those are on special lines? (1,6)-(2,6) horizontal, (1,6)-(1,7) vertical, (2,6)-(1,7) slope -1. So indeed not sunny. So that works.

Now consider a different selection, maybe using some slope -1 lines and some horizontals, but still achieving union size 25? For n=7, the maximum union with 5 lines is 25. That's achieved by taking 5 lines from a single family. Could there be a mix that also achieves 25? For instance, take 4 horizontals (y=1-4) and 1 vertical (x=1). Let's compute union size for n=7 with T = {y=1..4, x=1}. The horizontals y=1..4 have sizes 7,6,5,4 = 22. x=1 has size 7. Overlaps: each of y1..y4 intersect x1 at (1,1)-(1,4) → 4 overlaps. So union = 22+7-4 = 25. So that's also 25. So indeed a mix can achieve maximum. In that case, U would be the points not covered by those lines. Let's compute for n=7, T = {y=1,2,3,4, x=1}. Points P for n=7: a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 8. That's 1+2+...+7 = 28 points. The union of T covers many. We can compute U. The points covered are all points with y=1..4 (rows) and all points with x=1 (column). The uncovered points are those with y≥5 and x≥2. Also need to consider points with y=1..4 but x≥2 are covered by y rows. Points with x=1 and y≥1 are covered by column. So uncovered are points with x≥2, y≥5, and also a+b≤8. Let's list such points: For y=5, a≤3 (since 5+3=8) → (2,5),(3,5). For y=6, a≤2 → (2,6). For y=7, a≤1 → (1,7) but (1,7) has x=1, covered by column. So (2,5),(3,5),(2,6). That's 3 points. Check if they are covered by T? y=5 is not in T, so (2,5) is not covered by any y row (since y=5 not used), not covered by column (x=1). So uncovered. So U = {(2,5),(3,5),(2,6)}. Are any two of these on a sunny line? (2,5)-(3,5) horizontal (y=5) → not sunny. (2,5)-(2,6) vertical (x=2) → not sunny. (3,5)-(2,6) slope (6-5)/(2-3) = 1/(-1) = -1 → not sunny. So again no sunny line covers two. So the pattern holds.

Thus it appears that for any selection of m = n-2 special lines, the complement U is a set of points that are all in the "upper right" region (or "lower left"? Actually it's the complement of the union of some lines. The structure of U may vary, but we can try to prove that U is always a subset of the set of points that are not on any line of special direction, but we need to bound the number of sunny lines needed to cover U. Maybe we can prove that |U| ≥ 3 and that any line that contains two points of U must be a special direction. However, is it possible that U contains three points where two are on a sunny line? For that to happen, U must contain two points that are not on any of the chosen special lines. But if those two points are on a sunny line, then that sunny line would be a candidate. But would that cause a contradiction with the covering? Let's think: U is the set of points not covered by the special lines. So if two points in U happen to be collinear on a sunny line, then that sunny line is not one of the special lines, and it passes through those two points. That's allowed. The question is whether we can cover U with only 2 sunny lines. For k=2, we have exactly 2 sunny lines. If U has size 3, we could use one sunny line covering two points and another covering the third. That would be 2 lines. So we need to check whether there exists a selection of n-2 special lines such that U is of size 3 and two of those points lie on a sunny line. That would be a counterexample to the claim that k=2 is impossible. But from our examples, we haven't seen such U. But we need to examine if it's possible.

Consider n=6, m=4. The maximum union is 18, so U size at least 3. The possible U sets that arise from maximum-union selections are those that are complements of unions of 4 special lines that achieve maximum. We saw several such sets: e.g., after taking 4 horizontals (y=1-4), U = {(1,5),(2,5),(1,6)}. After taking y=1-3 and x=1, U = {(2,4),(2,5),(3,4)}. After taking 4 verticals, U = {(1,5),(2,5),(3,5),(4,5)}? Actually for n=6, verticals x=1-4 have sizes 6,5,4,3 = 18, complement is points with x≥5 and y≥? Let's compute. For n=6, verticals x=1..4: x1 (6), x2 (5), x3 (4), x4 (3) = 18. The uncovered points are those with x≥5 and y≥? That's (5,1),(5,2),(6,1) maybe? Actually list points with x≥5: (5,1) sum6, (5,2) sum7>6? Wait n+1=7, so (5,2) sum7 is allowed, yes. Also (6,1) sum7. So U = {(5,1),(5,2),(6,1)}. That's 3 points. Are any two on a sunny line? (5,1)-(5,2) vertical; (5,1)-(6,1) horizontal; (5,2)-(6,1) slope -1. So not sunny. So again no.

Thus it seems that all maximum-union selections lead to U being a set of 3 points that are "border" points, and any two are on a non-sunny line. But we should confirm that any set of 4 special lines that achieves union 18 (max) must be such that the uncovered points are a "triangle" of 3 points that are the "far" points. We can attempt to characterize the maximum union size and the shape of the complement. Since the total points are n(n+1)/2, and the maximum union from m lines is achieved by taking the m largest lines from a single family. But as we saw, mixing can also achieve maximum. So we need a more systematic argument: For any set T of m special lines, |∪T| ≤ m(2n - m + 1)/2. Equality occurs iff T consists of lines that are all from the same family and are the m largest lines in that family, OR possibly also some combinations that happen to have the same sum. But we should examine the structure of the bound. The bound derived by considering the sizes of lines and overlaps is not trivial, but we can prove a stronger bound: For any selection of m lines (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1), the number of points covered is at most (n + (n-1) + ... + (n-m+1)) = m(2n - m + 1)/2. This seems plausible because the maximum coverage is achieved by taking the m largest lines from a single family. But is it always true that any selection of m lines from the three families covers at most that many points? Let's try to prove.

Let T be a set of m lines, each is of type H (y = c), V (x = c), or S (x+y = c). For each such line L, let size(L) be the number of points from P that lie on L. The union size is at most sum_{L∈T} size(L) minus overlaps. But we cannot directly bound sum size(L) because overlaps can be large. However we can bound the union by the sum of the sizes of the m lines with the largest possible sizes, but that's not rigorous unless we argue that any line's size is ≤ n, and that if we have overlaps, the union is smaller. But we need an upper bound on union, not a lower bound. For proving that U is large, we need a lower bound on complement, i.e., an upper bound on union. So we need to show that |∪T| ≤ H_m = sum of the m largest sizes from a family. But is that always true? Consider T consisting of 2 lines: a horizontal y=1 (size n) and a slope -1 line x+y=n+1 (size n). Their union size is 2n-1 (since they intersect at (1,n) maybe). That's ≤ n + (n-1) = 2n-1 (since the two largest horizontals sum to 2n-1). But if we take two horizontals y=1 and y=2, that's n + (n-1) = 2n-1, which is the same. So in that case, union ≤ H_2. For three lines, maybe we can exceed H_3? For n=6, H_3 = 6+5+4 = 15. The union of three horizontals y=1-3 is 15. The union of two horizontals and one vertical might be less than 15. So H_m seems like an upper bound, but is it always an upper bound? Consider m lines where some are from different families, but they are all large. For example, n=6,m=3. Could we get union > 15? Now we need to produce a rigorous solution. Let's outline:

1. Define P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^+ × ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ n+1}. |P_n| = n(n+1)/2.

2. Let S be a set of n distinct lines L_1,…,L_n in the plane such that for each (a,b)∈P_n, there exists i with (a,b)∈L_i. Moreover, exactly k of these lines are sunny. We need to find all k (depending on n) for which such S exists.

We will prove:

- If k = 0 or k = 1, then such S exists. (Construct)
- If 3 ≤ k ≤ n-1, then such S exists.
- If k = 2 or k = n, then such S does not exist.

We'll need to handle n≥3.

Since translation is an isometry, covering U with lines is equivalent to covering T_t with lines that are also translated. But the lines we use must be distinct and sunny. If we have a set of t lines L_i that cover T_t (all points in T_t), then translating those lines by (0, n - t) yields a set of t lines that cover U. However are those translated lines sunny? The property of being sunny depends on the line's slope and orientation; translation does not change slope. So if the original lines are sunny (slope not 0,∞,-1), then the translated lines are also sunny. So if we can cover T_t with t sunny lines, then we can cover U with t sunny lines. So the problem reduces to: For any integer t ≥ 3, does there exist a set of t distinct sunny lines (none parallel to axes or slope -1) that cover all points in the triangle T_t = {(a,b)∈ℕ^+×ℕ^+ : a+b ≤ t+1}? (Note: T_t has size t(t+1)/2). Then we can use that as the sunny lines for the construction with m = n - t non-sunny horizontals (y=1,…,m). That would give a set of n lines with exactly k = t sunny lines, covering P_n. So we need to construct such a covering for any t ≥ 3. That seems plausible.

Now, we can generalize: For any t ≥ 3, we can cover the triangle T_t (points with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ t+1) with t sunny lines. But we need an explicit construction. The covering we gave for t=3 is not of the form "lines of slope 1 with different intercepts" (that would be y = x + c). However we used lines with slopes 1, -2, -1/2. That's a specific set. For t=4, we can try to find a covering with 4 sunny lines. Let's attempt to cover T_4 (points with a+b ≤ 5). T_4 has 10 points. We can try to use lines that are "semi-diagonals" with slopes that are rational numbers. Perhaps we can use the following general construction: For each i from 1 to t, define L_i to be the line passing through (i, i+1) and (i+1, i+2) which is slope 1. That's only one line. Not enough. But we can use lines that are "parallel to the line y = x" with different intercepts, but as we saw, those are all sunny and can cover many points, but not all of T_t. However we can combine them with some lines of other slopes. The covering we gave for t=3 is not a family of parallel lines. So we need a systematic method.

Observation: The uncovered triangle U after taking m smallest horizontals is a right triangle with legs of length t. Its points are all of the form (a,b) with a ∈ [1,t], b ∈ [n - t + 1, n] and a + b ≤ n+1. After translation, it becomes T_t. So we need to cover T_t with t sunny lines. We already have a covering for t=3. For t=4, we could try to find a covering by using lines of slopes 1, -2, -1/2, and maybe something like -3 or -1/3, etc. The pattern might be: For t, take lines L_i: y = - (i) x + something? Not sure.

Alternatively, we could use a different set of non-sunny lines that leave a different uncovered triangle that is easier to cover. For instance, we could take the non-sunny lines to be the t smallest vertical lines (x=1,…,t). Then the uncovered points are those with x > t. That's a triangle of size (n-t)(n-t+1)/2, but with coordinates shifted. That's similar. So we need a covering of a right triangle of size t with t lines. Maybe we can cover it with t lines that are all of the form y = c x + d, where c is something like 1,2,3,… but we need to ensure that each point is covered. This is reminiscent of covering the triangular grid with lines of different slopes. Perhaps we can use the following construction: For each i = 1,2,…,t, let L_i be the line that passes through (i, t+1) and (i+1, t+2) but we need to adjust. That seems like a diagonal.

Another idea: Use the lines that are the "rows" of the triangle after a certain transformation. Since we can choose the sunny lines arbitrarily, we could use lines that are all parallel to each other (like slope 1) but with different intercepts, and then cover the remaining points with some additional lines. But we only have t sunny lines, and we need to cover a triangle of size t(t+1)/2. If we use t lines of slope 1, each line covers about t/2 points (since the line y = x + c intersects P_t in about floor((t+1 - c)/2) points, which for c around 0 to t-1 gives numbers like t/2, t/2-1, …). The total coverage of t such lines would be about t^2/2, which is exactly the size of T_t, but they might not be disjoint (they are parallel, so disjoint). However the sum of the sizes of those t lines, if we take c = 0,1,…,t-1, would be sum_{c=0}^{t-1} floor((t+1 - c)/2). Let's compute. That sum is something like: for t odd, it's (t^2)/2? Let's test t=3: c=0 gives floor(4/2)=2, c=1 gives floor(3/2)=1, c=2 gives floor(2/2)=1. Sum = 4. But T_3 size is 6. So not enough. If we take c = -t+1,...,0, that would be negative differences, but those lines also cover points. So using slope 1 lines, we can cover points with differences ranging from -t+1 to t-1. That's 2t-1 lines, but we only have t lines. So we need a different approach.

Let's consider a general covering with t lines. The example t=3 uses three lines with slopes 1, -2, -1/2. That's a set of lines that are not all parallel, but they cover the triangle. Could we describe such a covering for any t? Maybe we can use lines of slopes 1, -2, -1/2, -3, -1/3, ... but we need to cover all points. The triangle T_t can be partitioned into "layers" corresponding to the value of (a+b) (which is constant along lines of slope -1). However we need lines that are not special. Perhaps we can use the following construction: For each i from 1 to t, take the line L_i that passes through (i, i+1) and (i+1, i+2). That's slope 1. That's not enough. But maybe we can take L_i: y = x + (t+1 - i) for i = 1..t. That's t lines with slope 1, covering points with differences from 1 to t. That covers points where b - a is between 1 and t. In T_t, points with b - a ≤ 0 are not covered. So we need to cover those with additional lines. But we have t lines total. So we could use some lines with negative slopes to cover those.

Alternative approach: Instead of using m smallest horizontals, we could use m smallest verticals, but that's symmetric. The challenge is to cover a right triangle of size t with t sunny lines. For t=3, we succeeded. For t=4, we need to see if it's possible. If it's not possible, then our claim that all k from 3 to n-1 are possible might be false for some n. But maybe we can use a different set of non-sunny lines that leave a different uncovered triangle that is easier to cover. For example, we could take the non-sunny lines to be some mixture that leaves a small uncovered region, but we have only m non-sunny lines. The uncovered region after taking m lines will be the complement of the union of those lines. To minimize the uncovered region, we would want the m lines to cover as many points as possible. The maximum union size from m lines is H_m. If we take m = n - k, then the uncovered region size is n(n+1)/2 - H_m. For k between 3 and n-1, m ≤ n-3. Let's compute the uncovered size for m = n - t (where t = k). That's C = n(n+1)/2 - (n - t)(2n - (n - t) + 1)/2 = n(n+1)/2 - (n - t)(n + t + 1)/2? Let's compute: m = n - t, then the sum of m largest horizontals (or verticals) is (n - t)(2n - (n - t) + 1)/2 = (n - t)(n + t + 1)/2. So C = [n(n+1) - (n - t)(n + t + 1)] / 2. Expand: n(n+1) = n^2 + n. (n - t)(n + t + 1) = (n - t)(n + t + 1) = (n)(n + t + 1) - t(n + t + 1) = n^2 + n t + n - t n - t^2 - t = n^2 + n - t^2 - t. Wait compute carefully:

(n - t)*(n + t + 1) = (n - t)*(n + t) + (n - t)*1 = (n^2 - t^2) + (n - t) = n^2 - t^2 + n - t.

Thus C = [ (n^2 + n) - (n^2 - t^2 + n - t) ] / 2 = [ n^2 + n - n^2 + t^2 - n + t ] / 2 = [ t^2 + t ] / 2 = t(t+1)/2.

So indeed, if we take the m largest horizontals (or verticals), the uncovered region has size t(t+1)/2. That's the same as the size of T_t. So that's good.

But note: In our earlier construction for n=6,k=3 (t=3), we used m=3 non-sunny lines that were the smallest horizontals (y=1,2,3), not the largest. That's because we wanted to cover the bottom rows, leaving a small triangle at the top. That's also a maximum-union set, but with different ordering. The size of the union of the m smallest horizontals (y=1..m) is also H_m, because those rows are disjoint and have sizes n, n-1, ..., n-m+1. That's the same as the m largest rows (y=n, n-1, ..., n-m+1) because the sizes are decreasing. So either set gives union size H_m. So we can choose whichever is convenient. In the n=6,k=3 example, we used y=1,2,3 (smallest) as non-sunny, leaving the top triangle (y≥4) of size 6. That's exactly the same as using y=4,5,6 as non-sunny would leave bottom triangle of size 6. So both are valid. So we have flexibility.

Thus, for any t ≥ 3, we need to cover T_t (the triangle of points with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ t+1) with t sunny lines. But we already have an example for t=3. For t=4, we need to find a covering. Let's attempt to construct one.

We can try to use the pattern: For t, take the lines L_i: y = - (i) x + something, but we need to cover all points. Perhaps we can cover T_t by taking lines that are the "rows" of the triangle after a certain transformation. However, we are not required to cover T_4 with 4 sunny lines; we can choose a different set of non-sunny lines that leaves a different uncovered triangle that is not T_4. For instance, we could take m = n - k non-sunny lines that are not all from the same family, but a mixture that results in a smaller uncovered region. But the maximum union size with m lines is H_m, which yields uncovered size t(t+1)/2. That's the best we can do to minimize uncovered region. If we use a different selection, the uncovered region may be larger, making it harder to cover with k sunny lines. So for covering, we want the uncovered region to be as small as possible, so that the sunny lines have fewer points to cover. Therefore, the optimal construction is to use m non-sunny lines that achieve maximum union size, i.e., H_m. That's what we considered. So we need to cover a triangle of size t(t+1)/2 with t sunny lines. That's a necessary condition for existence of a configuration with exactly k = t sunny lines, using m = n - t non-sunny lines that are optimal. But maybe we can use a different family (like slope -1 lines) to achieve a different uncovered triangle that is easier to cover? Let's explore.

We have three families. If we take m non-sunny lines all from the family of slope -1 (i.e., lines of the form x+y = c), then they are parallel and disjoint. Their sizes are 1,2,...,n. Taking the m largest such lines (c = n+1, n, ..., n-m+2) gives union size H_m. The uncovered points are those with x+y ≤ n+1 but not on those lines. That's a triangle at the lower left. That triangle is similar to T_m, but with coordinates shifted. That triangle also has m(m+1)/2 points. So the uncovered region is T_m (size m(m+1)/2) after translation. So that's the same.

Thus, for any t, the uncovered triangle after using m = n - t non-sunny lines (optimal) is a triangle of size t(t+1)/2. So to construct for k = t, we need to cover that triangle with t sunny lines. So the question reduces to: For which t ≥ 3 does there exist a set of t distinct sunny lines that cover all points in T_t (the triangle of points with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ t+1)? If for some t it's impossible, then our construction for that k fails. But maybe we can still construct for that k by using a different number of non-sunny lines (i.e., not optimal) that yields a different uncovered region that is easier to cover. But we have m = n - k non-sunny lines. Since we want to cover P_n with n lines, we can choose which lines are non-sunny. The uncovered region after those lines is U. We have k sunny lines to cover U. If we choose a suboptimal set of non-sunny lines, U becomes larger, making covering harder. So the best chance is to use optimal non-sunny lines. So if even optimal fails, then that k is impossible. However, we might also consider using some lines that are not all from the same family, and maybe a combination of families could yield a smaller uncovered region than H_m? But we already used the maximum union bound; the union size cannot exceed H_m for m lines, but could it be that H_m is not achievable for certain n? We computed that the sum of the m largest lines from a single family is H_m, and that's achievable. So that's the maximum. So any set of m lines covers at most H_m points. So the uncovered points are at least total - H_m. For m = n - t, that's t(t+1)/2. So U has at least t(t+1)/2 points. If we use a set of non-sunny lines that covers exactly H_m points (maximum), then U is exactly t(t+1)/2 points. If we cover fewer, U is larger. So the minimal possible U is t(t+1)/2. Therefore, to cover U with k = t sunny lines, we need to cover a set of size at least t(t+1)/2. Since we have t lines, the maximum total coverage (union) from t sunny lines is at most something, but they could cover many points. However, we are not forced to use all lines as sunny; we have m non-sunny lines. The question is whether there exists a set of n lines (some sunny) such that the union covers P_n. For a given k, we can attempt to design a set where the non-sunny lines are optimal (max coverage) and then the sunny lines cover the remaining. That seems the most promising. So we need to check for which t we can cover T_t with t sunny lines. If for some t it's impossible, then that k is not realizable. But is it possible that we could use a different arrangement where the non-sunny lines are not all from one family, but a mixture that leads to a smaller uncovered region (maybe less than t(t+1)/2) due to overlaps? But we already argued that the union size cannot exceed H_m, so the uncovered region cannot be smaller than total - H_m. That's a lower bound on uncovered size. So the uncovered region has at least t(t+1)/2 points. It could be larger. So to have a chance, we need to cover that uncovered region with k sunny lines. If we could cover a set of size L with k sunny lines, then any uncovered region of size ≤ L is possible. But we don't know the maximum coverage of k sunny lines. We need to determine if it's possible to cover a triangle of size t(t+1)/2 with t sunny lines. That's the best case. If it's impossible, maybe we could cover with a different set of non-sunny lines that cover slightly fewer points, making U larger, but then we have more sunny lines? Wait we have exactly n lines. But we need to check if those lines cover all points. Let's try to construct lines for T_5 based on that set. For each slope, we can choose a line that passes through some points.
