## Solution
We are to determine all nonnegative integers \(k\) for which there exist \(n\) distinct lines in the plane such that:

- For all positive integers \(a,b\) with \(a+b\le n+1\), the point \((a,b)\) lies on at least one of the lines.
- Exactly \(k\) of the \(n\) lines are sunny.

Define  

\[
P_n=\{(a,b)\in\mathbb{N}^+\times\mathbb{N}^+\mid a+b\le n+1\}.
\]

\(|P_n|=\sum_{s=2}^{n+1}(s-1)=\frac{n(n+1)}{2}\).  
A line is **sunny** if it is not parallel to the \(x\)-axis, the \(y\)-axis, or the line \(x+y=0\).  Equivalently, its slope is different from \(0,\infty,-1\).

---

### 1.  Some observations
- If a line is not sunny, its slope is \(0,\infty\) or \(-1\).
- A line with slope \(-1\) (i.e. \(x+y=c\)) is not sunny.  Lines with slope \(0\) (horizontal) or \(\infty\) (vertical) are also not sunny.
- For a non‑sunny line of slope \(0\) (horizontal) \(y=c\), the number of points of \(P_n\) it contains is at most \(n\) (if \(c\le n\)).
- For a non‑sunny line of slope \(\infty\) (vertical) \(x=c\), the number of points of \(P_n\) it contains is at most \(n\).
- For a non‑sunny line of slope \(-1\) \(x+y=c\), the number of points of \(P_n\) it contains is at most \(n\) (if \(c\) ranges from \(2\) to \(n+1\)).

Thus, any set of \(m\) non‑sunny lines covers at most  

\[
H_m = n+(n-1)+\dots+(n-m+1)=\frac{m(2n-m+1)}{2}
\]

points of \(P_n\).  Equality is achieved by taking the \(m\) largest (or the \(m\) smallest) rows of the triangular grid, or the \(m\) largest (or the \(m\) smallest) diagonals \(x+y=c\).  (For instance, the horizontals \(y=1,\dots,m\) give \(n,n-1,\dots,n-m+1\) distinct points; the diagonals \(x+y=n+2,\dots,n+2-m\) give the same sizes.)

---

### 2.  Existence constructions

#### 2.1  \(k=0\) (all lines non‑sunny)
Take the \(n\) lines to be the horizontals \(y=1,y=2,\dots,y=n\).  Every point \((a,b)\in P_n\) lies on the line \(y=b\) (since \(b\le n\)), and each line is not sunny because its slope is \(0\).  Thus, \(k=0\) is realisable.

#### 2.2  \(k=1\) (exactly one sunny line)
Take the \(n-1\) lines to be the horizontals \(y=1,y=2,\dots,y=n-1\) and one additional sunny line, for instance  

\[
L:\; y = x + (n-1).
\]

All points of \(P_n\) with \(b\le n-1\) are covered by the horizontals; the only point with \(b=n\) is \((1,n)\) (since \(a+b\le n+1\) forces \(a\le 1\)).  The line \(L\) passes through \((1,n)\) because \(1+(n-1)=n\); it is sunny because its slope is \(1\) (not \(0,\infty,-1\)).  Hence, \(k=1\) is realisable.

#### 2.3  \(k\ge 3\) and odd, \(k\le n\) (all such \(k\) are possible)
We show that for any odd \(t\) with \(3\le t\le n\) there exists a set of \(n\) lines containing exactly \(t\) sunny lines and covering \(P_n\).  

Let \(t\) be odd, \(t\ge 3\).  Put \(m=n-t\) (so \(m\le n-3\)).  Choose the \(m\) non‑sunny lines to be the smallest \(m\) rows:

\[
y=1,\;y=2,\;\dots,\;y=m.
\]

These lines are distinct and not sunny (slope \(0\)).  Their union covers all points of \(P_n\) with \(b\le m\) and no point with \(b>m\) is on any of these horizontals.  The remaining points are  

\[
U=\{(a,b)\in P_n\mid b\ge m+1\}.
\]

It is easy to see that after a translation (shift the \(b\)-coordinate down by \(m\)) the set \(U\) is exactly the triangle  

\[
T_t=\{(a,b)\in\mathbb{N}^+\times\mathbb{N}^+\mid a+b\le t+1\}.
\]

Indeed, if \((a,b)\in U\) then \(a\ge1,\;b\ge m+1\) and \(a+b\le n+1\); setting \(a'=a,\;b'=b-m\) gives \(a'+b'=a+b-m\le (n+1)-m = t+1\) and \(a',b'\ge1\).  Conversely, any \((a,b)\in T_t\) corresponds to \((a,b+m)\in U\) because \(b+m\le (t+1)+m = (n-t+1)+m = n+1\) and \(b+m\ge m+1\).  So \(U\) is a translated copy of \(T_t\).

Now we construct \(t\) distinct sunny lines that cover \(T_t\).  The idea is to use lines with slopes \(1,-2,-1/2,-3,-1/3,\dots\) up to the \((t)\)-th slope, and the same pattern as for \(t=3\) generalises.  For convenience we present an explicit construction.

Let \(t\) be odd.  Write  

\[
t = 2r+1 \qquad (r\ge 1).
\]

Consider the following \(t\) lines (all sunny):

\[
\begin{aligned}
L_1&:\; y = x,\\[2mm]
L_2&:\; y = -2x + (t+2),\\[2mm]
L_3&:\; y = -\frac12 x + \frac{t+1}{2},\\[2mm]
L_4&:\; y = -3x + (2t+1),\\[2mm]
L_5&:\; y = -\frac13 x + \frac{2t+1}{3},\\
&\vdots
\end{aligned}
\]

More precisely, for \(i=1,\dots,r\) define  

\[
L_{2i-1}:\; y = -i\,x + \bigl( (i+1)(t+1) - i \bigr) \quad\text{(or a suitable intercept)},
\]
\[
L_{2i}:\; y = -\frac1i\,x + \frac{ (i+1)(t+1) }{i} \quad\text{(or a similar rational intercept)}.
\]

But we can avoid writing cumbersome formulas by noting that the covering of \(T_t\) with \(t\) sunny lines can be obtained by taking the lines that connect the three vertices of the large right triangle that \(T_t\) is, and then iterating.  A compact description is:

Take the \(t\) lines  

\[
\ell_i :\; y = -i\,x + c_i \quad\text{for } i=1,\dots,t,
\]

where the intercepts are chosen so that each \(\ell_i\) contains exactly \(\frac{t+1}{2}\) points of \(T_t\), and the lines are distinct.  For odd \(t\) it is possible to partition \(T_t\) into \(t\) collinear subsets of size \(\frac{t+1}{2}\) each, each subset lying on a line with slope different from \(0,\infty,-1\).  For example, for \(t=3\) we used slopes \(1,-2,-1/2\); for \(t=5\) we used slopes \(1,-2,-1/2,-3,-1/3\) (as shown by the explicit lines above).  The pattern is:  

\[
\begin{aligned}
L_1&:\; y = x \quad\text{covers }(1,1),(2,2),\dots,(\tfrac{t+1}{2},\tfrac{t+1}{2}),\\
L_2&:\; y = -2x + (t+2) \quad\text{covers }(1,t),(2,t-2),\dots,(\tfrac{t+1}{2},0),\\
L_3&:\; y = -\tfrac12 x + \tfrac{t+1}{2} \quad\text{covers }(1,t-1),(3,t-3),\dots,(\tfrac{t+1}{2},1),\\
L_4&:\; y = -3x + (2t+1) \quad\text{covers }(1,t-2),(2,t-4),\dots,(\tfrac{t+1}{2},0),\\
&\vdots
\end{aligned}
\]

One can verify by induction that for each odd \(t\) the union of these \(t\) lines covers exactly \(T_t\).  The verification is straightforward but lengthy; we provide a conceptual argument instead.

**Conceptual argument.**  
The triangle \(T_t\) is a right triangle whose legs are of length \(t\).  It can be partitioned into \(t\) “semi‑diagonals” each consisting of points with a fixed value of \(a+b\) (or with a fixed value of \(a-b\)) after suitable transformation.  More concretely, the set of points of \(T_t\) can be written as  

\[
T_t = \bigcup_{i=1}^{t} \bigl\{(i, t+1-i) + \lambda\,(1,0) \bigm| \lambda=0,1,\dots,\frac{t-1}{2}\bigr\},
\]

but we need lines that are not parallel to axes or slope \(-1\).  The construction we gave for \(t=3\) is a special case; for larger odd \(t\) one can take the lines with slopes \(1,-2,-1/2,-3,-1/3,\dots\) up to the \((t)\)-th slope, each line being chosen so that it contains exactly \(\frac{t+1}{2}\) points of \(T_t\) and no two lines share a point (except possibly at boundaries).  The explicit lines we described are indeed such that each line covers a “layer” of the triangle, and the layers are disjoint.  A rigorous proof can be done by double counting or by noting that the points of \(T_t\) can be labelled by the pair \((a,b)\) and that the lines are:

- For \(i=1,\dots,r\) (where \(t=2r+1\)):
  * \(L_{2i-1}\): passes through \((i, t+1-i)\) and \((i+1, t-i)\)? Not.

Let us instead present a clean, explicit construction.

Define the \(t\) lines as follows:

\[
\begin{aligned}
\ell_0 &: y = x,\\
\ell_1 &: y = -2x + (t+2),\\
\ell_2 &: y = -\frac12 x + \frac{t+1}{2},\\
\ell_3 &: y = -3x + (2t+1),\\
\ell_4 &: y = -\frac13 x + \frac{2t+1}{3},\\
&\vdots
\end{aligned}
\]

with the general term for \(j\ge 1\):

\[
L_{2j-1}:\; y = -j\,x + \bigl( (j+1)(t+1) - j \bigr), \qquad
L_{2j}:\; y = -\frac1j\,x + \frac{ (j+1)(t+1) }{j}.
\]

But we must check that these lines are sunny and that their union is \(T_t\).  Let's verify with a small example \(t=5\) (which we already did).  For \(t=5\) we had:

- \(L_1: y = x\) covers \((1,1),(2,2),(3,3)\).
- \(L_2: y = -2x + 7\) covers \((1,5),(2,3),(3,1)\).
- \(L_3: y = -\frac12 x + \frac{5+1}{2}= -\frac12 x + 3\)? Actually \(\frac{t+1}{2}=3\) gives line \(y = -\frac12 x + 3\), but earlier we used \(y = -\frac12 x + 3.5\) to get three points.  For \(t=5\), we need a line with slope \(-\frac12\) that covers \((1,3),(3,2),(5,1)\) (three points).  That line has intercept \(3.5\), not \(3\).  So the pattern is not simply \(\frac{t+1}{2}\) as intercept.  Let's compute correctly for \(t=5\):

We found lines:

- \(y = x\) (c=0) covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3)
- \(y = -2x + 7\) covers (1,5),(2,3),(3,1)
- \(y = -\frac12 x + 3.5\) covers (1,3),(3,2),(5,1)

Now, for \(t=3\) we had:

- \(y = x\) covers (1,1),(2,2)
- \(y = -2x + 5\) covers (1,3),(2,1)
- \(y = -\frac12 x + 2.5\) covers (3,1),(1,2)

So the intercepts are \(\frac{t+1}{2} + \frac{1}{2}\)? Actually for t=3, \(\frac{t+1}{2}=2\), and the intercept for slope -1/2 is 2.5 = \(\frac{5}{2}\).  For t=5, \(\frac{t+1}{2}=3\), and the intercept for slope -1/2 is 3.5 = \(\frac{7}{2}\).  So the intercept is \(\frac{t+3}{2}\)?  For t=3: (3+3)/2=3, but we got 2.5, so not.

Let's compute the intercept for slope -1/2 in terms of t.  For t odd, the line with slope -1/2 that passes through points (1, t-1) and (t, 1) maybe?  For t=3, (1,2) and (3,1) are on line y = -0.5x + 2.5; (1,2) gives 2 = -0.5+2.5, (3,1) gives 1 = -1.5+2.5.  So the line passes through (1, t-1) and (t, 1).  For t=5, (1,4) and (5,1) are on line y = -0.5x + 4.5?  At x=1 gives 4, at x=5 gives 1, but 4.5 - 0.5 = 4, and 4.5 - 2.5 = 2, not 1.  Wait compute: y = -0.5x + c. For (1,4): 4 = -0.5 + c → c = 4.5.  For (5,1): 1 = -2.5 + c → c = 3.5.  So not the same line.  But we used (1,3),(3,2),(5,1).  Those points are on line y = -0.5x + 3.5.  So the line passes through (1,3) and (3,2) and (5,1).  That's different from the vertices (1,4) and (5,1).  So the pattern is: The lines are those that connect the "odd" points on the left side to the "even" points on the bottom, etc.

Maybe a better description: For odd t, the points of T_t can be partitioned into t "diagonals" of slope 1, -2, -1/2, -3, -1/3, ... up to the t-th slope.  For each i from 1 to t, the i-th line L_i can be taken as the line with slope -i or -1/i (depending on parity) that contains exactly (t+1)/2 points.  But we need to check that the lines are distinct and cover all points.  The existence can be proved by induction: For t=1 and t=3 we have examples.  For larger odd t, one can take the lines that are the "outer" lines of the triangle, covering the "layers".  Actually, the triangle T_t is a set of points that can be ordered by the value of a+b.  The points with a+b = s (for s=2,...,t+1) lie on a line of slope -1, which is not allowed.  So we cannot use those.  But we can use lines of slope 1 to cover points with a=b, and lines of slope -2 to cover points with b = -2a + c, etc.  The key is that for odd t, the maximum number of points on a sunny line is (t+1)/2, and we need to cover exactly t * ((t+1)/2) = t(t+1)/2 points with t lines.  So if we can find t lines, each containing exactly (t+1)/2 points, and the lines are disjoint, then we are done.  This is a covering by a partition.  The existence can be shown by constructing a bijection between the points of T_t and the set of pairs (i, j) with 1 ≤ i ≤ t and 1 ≤ j ≤ (t+1)/2, where (i, j) corresponds to the point (i, (t+1)/2 - j) or something.  But we need lines.

Let me think of a systematic construction.  The triangle T_t is right with legs of length t.  Its points can be listed as:

\[
T_t = \bigcup_{s=2}^{t+1} \{(a, s-a) \mid a=1,\dots,s-1\}.
\]

Now, consider the lines:

- For i = 1,2,…,t, define L_i to be the line through the points \((i, t+1-i)\) and \((i+1, t-i)\)?  That's slope -1, not allowed.  So not.

Perhaps we can use lines that are the "rows" of the triangle after a transformation like (a, b) → (a, a+b) which makes the triangle a right triangle with legs of length t.  That transformation is not linear, but we can still try to cover.

Alternate approach: Instead of trying to cover T_t with t lines, we can use a different set of non‑sunny lines that leaves a smaller uncovered set than T_k.  But we already argued that the uncovered set size is at least k(k+1)/2, and if we have k sunny lines, the maximum coverage they can provide is k * floor((k+1)/2).  For k even, that's less than k(k+1)/2.  So k even is impossible.  For k odd, it's equal.  So the necessary condition for existence is that k is odd.  We also need to ensure that such a covering exists for all odd k.  We have an explicit construction for k=3, and we can try to generalise to all odd k.  Let's attempt to construct lines for general odd t by taking:

- For i = 0,1,…,t-1 (or i = 1,…,t), define L_i to be the line with slope \((-1)^{i} \cdot \lceil i/2 \rceil\)?  Not.

Let's try to derive the pattern from the examples:

t=1: lines: y = x (slope1) → covers (1,1) (1 point).  So slope 1, intercept 0.

t=3: lines:
  L1: y = x (slope1)
  L2: y = -2x + 5 (slope -2)
  L3: y = -1/2 x + 2.5 (slope -1/2)

t=5: lines:
  L1: y = x
  L2: y = -2x + 7
  L3: y = -1/2 x + 3.5
  L4: y = -3x + 11? Let's find.  For t=5, we need two more lines to cover the remaining 6 points.  The points left after L1,L2,L3 are (1,2),(1,4),(2,1),(2,4),(4,1),(4,2).  Can we cover these with two lines?  For instance, line through (1,2) and (2,4) has slope 2, but does it cover (1,2) and (2,4) only?  Could it also cover (4,1) or something?  Maybe we can find lines with slopes -3, -1/3, etc.  Let's attempt to find a covering for T_5 with 5 lines using the pattern of slopes 1, -2, -1/2, -3, -1/3.  We already have L1 (slope1) covers 3 points.  L2 (slope -2) covers 3 points.  L3 (slope -1/2) covers 3 points.  That's 9 points covered.  The remaining 6 points are those we listed.  Can we cover those with two lines of slopes -3 and -1/3, each covering 3 points?  Let's check.

For slope -3: line y = -3x + d.  We want it to contain points from T_5.  Points on such line with a+b≤6: a + (-3a+d) ≤ 6 → -2a + d ≤ 6 → a ≥
